HEADLINE: State Oversight Urged as Way To Save Buffalo 

BYLINE:  By WINNIE HU 

DATELINE: ALBANY, May 28 

 BODY:
After years of teetering on the brink of bankruptcy, Buffalo is faced with having to turn over its financial operations to a state board that would oversee nearly all major city spending.
 The drastic prospect of a state financial control board taking over the finances of the city of nearly 300,000 was raised today by the state comptroller. Alan G. Hevesi, the comptroller, said that without such intervention, Buffalo and its schools could face a budget gap of between $93 million and $127 million in the 2006-2007 fiscal year. The overall budget for the city and its schools is currently about $925 million. 
 Buffalo, once a thriving, commercial hub for western New York, has experienced higher unemployment and seen residents leave the city in recent years, even during the boom of the late 1990's.
Each year, its government has spent more money than it has taken in, becoming dependent on state bailouts and borrowing to make ends meet.
The financial woes have become particularly acute in Buffalo's schools, which do not have independent taxing authority and must depend on the city for its revenue. About 1,400 positions -- or 28 percent of the district staff -- have been eliminated in three years. Buffalo schools have also talked about adopting a four-day week to save money, and the Buffalo Board of Education announced earlier this year that it would have to close at least two elementary schools and an alternative high school next month.
The proposal presented today by Mr. Hevesi was viewed by many Buffalo officials and residents as a lifeline for a city that has simply run out of financial options. While state aid to the city has more than doubled, to $103 million from $51 million, in less than a decade, state officials are now facing their own fiscal problems and have been less willing to bail out the city. 
Mr. Hevesi said that Sheldon Silver, the State Assembly speaker, asked him to review the city's finances this month.
"The point is not to blame anyone," said Mr. Hevesi, who added that the board was intended to "correct these problems for the long-term and get everybody at the table who is a stake-holder, and who has power and authority to help make the decisions and share the responsibility."
The proposal, which must be approvedby Gov. George E. Pataki and the State Legislature, would be the fifth time in state history that a financial control board has been created to oversee a city's finances. Similar financial control boards were put in place in New York City in 1975, in Yonkers in 1975 and again in 1984, and in Troy in 1994. In addition, the state also appointed a financial control board for Nassau County in 2000.
Under Mr. Hevesi's plan, the state would create a Buffalo Oversight and Recovery Board that would have to authorize any city expenditure of $50,000, or more, before the money could be disbursed. The bipartisan board, which would be chaired by Governor Pataki, would be made up of seven voting members, including Mr. Hevesi; Mr. Silver; Joseph L. Bruno, the Senate majority leader; and Anthony M. Masiello, Buffalo's mayor.
In addition, the financial control board would have five nonvoting members who would be drawn from the Buffalo city council and school board, the Erie County Legislature, and the business and labor communities. The board would review a four-year financial plan that would have to be submitted by Buffalo's mayor and its council; the board would also have access to the city's records.
In a written statement, Governor Pataki said today that he supports the idea of a financial control board for Buffalo, but he believed that its members should include more financial experts rather than elected officials. He pointed to Nassau's control board, saying that it "has succeeded because it placed politics aside and relied on the expertise of highly qualified financial experts."
Mr. Hevesi said that he stood by his proposed board, explaining that the situation was different because Nassau's economy was much stronger. "Buffalo needs a board that can agree to a recovery plan, including not just cuts and efficiencies, but also additional resources, and then make sure it is implemented," he said. "Elected officials, or their designees, have that power. Private individuals do not." 
Mr. Silver indicated his support today for a state board in Buffalo, and his aides said later that the speaker would be reviewing Mr. Hevesi's proposal with the Assembly members from that area. "I think there has to be something that brings the Buffalo finances under control, somebody has to overlook it," he said. "It is a situation that I think needs attention."
Mr. Bruno, who had not yet seen Mr. Hevesi's specific proposal, also said that a financial control board was probably needed in Buffalo, given that city's financial situation.
In Buffalo, Mayor Masiello held a news conference to pledge his support for the proposed state board, saying that it would provide him with "partners at the county, state and local level who have some real clout and can help give me the tools I need."
The mayor added: "We have an opportunity to get better, get healthier and deliver better services. It's going to take some pain and some tough love, but I'm up to the task."
The Erie County executive, Joel A. Giambra, who lives in Buffalo and previously served as the city comptroller, said that a state board was necessary because city leaders could not seem to solve the problem.
He blamed the city's financial crisis on the unwillingness of some of those leaders to make structural changes that would save money.
"They are more determined to throw money at the problem than to fix the problem," Mr. Giambra said. "That's a much easier solution, but it doesn't work. It hasn't worked in a decade." 
In the past month, the Buffalo Niagara Partnership, which represents more than 3,500 businesses, has sent letters to the governor and state legislators, requesting that a financial control board be appointed as soon as possible for the city "with as many sharp teeth as possible." 
Andrew J. Rudnick, president of the partnership, said that Mr. Hevesi's conclusions about the city's dismal financial prospects confirmed what many of his members have known for months.
"This is a step," he said. "It needs some fine-tuning, but we should be focusing on just getting it done."

HEADLINE: Troops and Police Clash With Protesters as Unrest Grows in Peru 

BYLINE:  Reuters

DATELINE: LIMA, Peru 

 BODY:
Soldiers fired into the air and police used tear gas and water hoses today in violent clashes across Peru with protesters who vowed to continue crippling strikes despite a government state of emergency.
 The police said at least seven farmers were wounded in Barranca, north of Lima, as soldiers fired shots to disperse rock-throwing protesters a day after President Alejandro Toledo imposed a 30-day state of emergency, banning strikers from the streets and giving the military and the police more powers to use force and conduct searches. 
 The police did not say how many people were arrested in the latest in a series of widespread protests by Peruvians demanding better pay and conditions. 
In the northern city of Chiclayo, security forces fired tear gas and arrested teachers, while shops in the jungle city of Huanuco were closed to avoid looting. In Lima, police in riot gear turned water hoses on protesting court workers at the national justice palace.
Health workers and farmers have, at least officially, temporarily called off strikes that disrupted highway transport with blockades of rocks and trees. But teachers, striking for more than two weeks demanding a raise of $60, to an average monthly wage of $200, said they were undeterred.
"The 100 sol ($29) raise they have offered us is insufficient," said Nilver Lopez, head of the Sutep union, which represents 280,000 teachers. "So we teachers have the right to keep expressing our unhappiness in the streets."
Earlier today, army troops backed by armored vehicles began clearing blocked highways. But the country's 1,500-mile Pan American Highway remained blocked in dozens of places by rocks and smoldering tires placed by angry farmers.
Thousands of travelers have been stranded since Monday as farmers sought to stop farm produce from getting to Lima and other cities.
Mr. Toledo declared the state of emergency on national television late Tuesday night, saying tolerance had its limit. "We have the responsibility to govern for 26 million Peruvians," he said. "We have the responsibility to protect citizens and the public order."
Mr. Toledo's two-year-old presidency has been marked by protests and a declining approval rating that now stands at 14 percent.
"One thing is democracy and another thing is the understanding that democracy means people can destroy the peace," Prime Minister Luis Solari told RPP radio.
Many Peruvians have complained that Toledo -- a former World Bank adviser -- has failed to fulfill ambitious promises of jobs, prosperity and a return to true democracy after the corrupt, hard-line rule of ex-President Alberto Fujimori.
Protests intensified in recent weeks with farmers, teachers, health and court workers pledging they would not give up on demands for wage rises and greater job security. 
Today, many schools appeared to remain empty despite a promise to reopen. "This isn't democracy. They send out soldiers as soon as they are unable to manage," said teacher Carmen Fajardo, 58, banging cymbals at a Lima school.
Mr. Toledo last declared a state of emergency in June 2002. That measure was limited to the city of Arequipa after three people were killed in protests there against the sale of two power firms.
Miguel Angel Bermudez, an analyst at private consultancy Maximixe in Lima, said the measure was a sign "of urgency, of the government's despair."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST 

 BODY:
Politicians Propose Limits On Rising Credit Card Rates
A provision now built into most credit card agreements allows issuers to reset a cardholder's interest rate based on the size and status of the person's other debts. And issuers check their customers' data regularly, not only when they review applications or notice missed payments.
 Concerned that consumers have not been sufficiently informed about the practice and that the sharp jumps in rates may be an overreaction, some state and federal legislators are proposing limits. Page A1. 

Suit Is Filed Over Executive Pay
A shareholder of John Hancock Financial Services filed a federal lawsuit over the compensation of the company's chief executive, David F. D'Alessandro, left, and other senior executives, seeking the return to the company of much of the $42.3 million they received. Mr. D'Alessandro's pay package rose to $21.7 million in 2002, up from $8.2 million in 2001. C4.

Bush Signs Tax Cut Bill Into Law
President Bush signed the third tax cut in three years into law, dismissing criticism of the cost, fairness and effectiveness and casting the measure as "essential action to strengthen the American economy." A18.

Samuel D. Waksal, the former chief executive of ImClone Systems who pleaded guilty to securities fraud, perjury and other charges last year, will be sentenced at a hearing June 10, the judge overseeing his case said. C5.

UAL Posts $375 Million Loss in April
UAL, parent of United Airlines, which is operating in bankruptcy, posted a $375 million net loss for April. C4.

Sony introduced a PlayStation 2 video game console that has a built-in DVD recorder and hard drive for storing data. C12.

Krispy Kreme Doughnuts said its first-quarter profit climbed 48 percent after sales surged and it opened more stores. The company raised its full-year earnings forecast. Net income increased to $13.1 million, or 22 cents a share, from $8.86 million, or 15 cents, a year earlier. Sales rose 34 percent, to $148.7 million. C2.

Big Korean Trading Firm to Be Shut
The creditors of SK Global, the trading firm at the center of a scandal that threatens to tear apart the South Korean conglomerate, rejected a plan by a sister company to prop the trading company up, and instead moved to shut it down. World Business, Section W.

Gucci to Distribute $1.58 Billion
Gucci will distribute $1.58 billion to shareholders in October to reduce the amount that its biggest shareholder will have to pay next year to buy the Gucci shares it does not already own. World Business, Section W.

Incentives, like Saddam Hussein's promised bounties, do little to spur people to become terrorists. A lack of civil liberties has far more effect. Alan B. Krueger: Economic Scene. C2.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING -- ADDENDA; 
Delta Drops Burnett In Move to Cut Costs

BYLINE:  By DANNY HAKIM

 BODY:
Delta Air Lines in Atlanta is making significant changes in its advertising, dismissing the creative agency for its flagship Delta brand while making assignments for its new low-fare carrier, Song.
 Yesterday, Delta dismissed Leo Burnett USA in Chicago, part of the Leo Burnett Worldwide division of the Publicis Groupe, which has handled creative duties on the Delta airline account since September 1999. 
 Most ad work for the Delta brand will now be handled internally, with help from BrightHouse in Atlanta, which has worked with Delta since August, to assist with creative and brand duties on a project basis.
Delta attributed the shift to "finding cost efficiencies."
The airline, like its competitors, has been struggling since the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks to cut spending as revenue falls and losses mount. Nina Avbee, executive vice president and group account director at Burnett, said the agency executives "entirely understand the pressures that Delta continues to face in this environment, and will do everything possible to make this a smooth transition."
Although billings for the Delta brand were not disclosed, ad spending in major media declined to $38 million last year from $57.1 million in 1999, according to TNS Media Intelligence/CMR, part of Taylor Nelson Sofres. Starcome Worldwide in Chicago, part of the Starcom MediaVest Group division of Publicis, will continue to handle media buying for Delta.
Song is hiring on a project basis several divisions of the Kirshenbaum Bond Creative Network in New York, including Kirshenbaum Bond & Partners for creative tasks; Media Kitchen for media services; and Lime Public Relations and Promotion for promotionalduties. Billings were estimated at $10 million to $12 million.

HEADLINE: A Better Class Of Competition 

BYLINE:  By JERE LONGMAN 

DATELINE: DETROIT 

 BODY:
A week before spring training ended, Jeremy Bonderman got the news that had seemed highly unlikely a few months earlier. He had made the Detroit Tigers' roster and would pitch the second game of the season.
 "You think it'd be O.K. if I went and called my dad?" Bonderman asked so earnestly that Bob Cluck, the Tigers' pitching coach, said he turned away as tears filled his own eyes. 
 Only two springs earlier, Bonderman was a junior in high school, pitching before several dozen fans and scouts in rural Washington State. Now, at age 20, after a season in Class A ball, he was headed for the big leagues. He still called his pitching coach Mister and wanted to know where he could get a sports coat required to ride the team charter. "I've never been to a big city," Bonderman confided to catcher Brandon Inge. 
Inge, sitting at his locker in the Tigers' clubhouse, said: "It's funny. A guy who's getting some wins in the big leagues can't even have a beer after the game."
Much attention has been paid to high school seniors who jump directly to professional basketball and to college athletes who leave school early to play for pay. But less has been said about another development: athletes who bypass their senior year of high school entirely and advance to college athletics or to the pros.
Bonderman, for example, became the first player selected in the baseball draft after his junior year of high school in June 2001. He skipped his senior year at Pasco High in Washington after receiving a G.E.D. certificate and turning 18, Major League Baseball's minimum age.
John David Booty, a quarterback from Shreveport, La., plans to graduate this summer and will skip his senior year at Evangel Christian Academy to enter the University of Southern California, where he will compete to replace Carson Palmer, last year's Heisman Trophy winner. 
Although the National Collegiate Athletic Association does not keep such records, Booty is believed to be the first Division I football player -- or at least the first prominent one -- to leave high school so early, football experts said. Another young athlete leaving high school early is Dana Hall, a 17-year-old soccer goalkeeper. She is graduating a year early from Kingwood High School in suburban Houston and will enter the University of Texas this fall. Coaches estimate that three or four of the best high school players in women's soccer take this accelerated route each year. The trend has included regulars on the United States national team like forwards Mia Hamm and Cindy Parlow and goalkeeper Siri Mullinix.
Supporters of early graduation say that athletes who take this path are highly motivated and disciplined and have matured faster than athletes in past decades because of their exposure to more sophisticated coaching and training methods and more advanced competition.
Those who question this athletic leapfrogging say that teenagers are missing a cherished year of high school and may be rushing into decisions -- or feeling pressure to make choices -- before they are physically and socially mature, and before they have explored a wider range of school choices.
Eligibility guidelines vary from sport to sport. For instance, athletes compete professionally at 14 in tennis and perform internationally at 15 in figure skating without having to meet any academic requirements. The N.C.A.A. requires collegiate athletes to be high school graduates who have taken a core curriculum of courses and have achieved a minimum grade point average and standardized test result based on a sliding scale. Major League Baseball has since closed the loophole that enabled Bonderman to be drafted as a junior; players must now exhaust their high school eligibility before entering the draft.
"We don't want to get into a situation of having people not complete their high school education," said Patrick Courtney, a spokesman for Major League Baseball.
It is too early to know whether early departure from high school will become a widespread trend, either through established rules or court challenges to existing regulations, but for years sports administrators have confronted an invasion of younger athletes seeking scholarships, Olympic medals and professional paychecks. A year ago, LeBron James, the prep basketball phenom from Akron, Ohio, considered leaving high school after his junior year and challenging the National Basketball Association's requirement that American-born players remain ineligible until their high school classes graduate.
The N.C.A.A. said its membership was split on the growing practice of high school football players who graduate a semester early in the winter so they can attend spring football practice in college. As for athletes graduating an entire year early and skipping their senior seasons, "It is on our radar screen," Kevin Lennon, an N.C.A.A. vice president, said.
"If our membership found that young people were coming to campus at an increasing rate who were unprepared, it would respond," Lennon said. "But the numbers aren't there to put it as a top priority."
Still, even those who have benefited from early high school graduation caution that the decision needs thorough examination beforehand and sound advice from adults acting in the athlete's best interest.
"Do some serious soul searching whether you are ready," said Hamm, who is soccer's leading international scorer and one of the world's most recognized athletes. "It has to be more than one level -- athletically, academically and socially. If you're not ready, you're going to stumble."

Jeremy Bonderman
Riding a Fastball To the Majors
Detroit's plan this season for Jeremy Bonderman was to send him to Class AA ball in Erie, Pa. Baseball is littered with the ruined careers of young pitchers called up too quickly to the majors. "It's always a fine line," Tigers Manager Alan Trammell said. "Are you going to destroy a kid's confidence?"
And yet Trammell sensed a maturity and determination in Bonderman, a 6-foot-1, 210-pound right-hander, that belied his age. Perhaps it was the 95-mile-an-hour fastball, the wicked slider or the tattoo of a flaming baseball on his left biceps. In any case, there would be little pressure for Bonderman to succeed immediately with the Tigers, who own baseball's worst record and are on pace to lose more than 100 games this season. Perhaps Detroit could speed his development by keeping him in the big leagues.
Bob Cluck, the pitching coach, convinced Bonderman that he could gain accuracy on his fastball by slowing the speed slightly. And Cluck moved him to the left side of the rubber to better situate his slider. After two labored starts, Bonderman began to find his rhythm. By mid-May, he led American League rookies in strikeouts, even though his 2-7 record suggests that his control grows elusive, his pitches dipping and darting like a kite on the wind.
"We feel strongly that he is going to be a very good pitcher for a long time," Trammell said. "He's not polished, but we feel the staff is helping him along."
Pitching is all he ever wanted to do, Bonderman said. His pale skin does not hint at his outdoor life in the tiny farm town of Burbank, Wash., where he played baseball and hunted ducks and worked in the fields of potatoes, asparagus, alfalfa and corn. During his junior year, he dropped out of Pasco High School, took an exam to acquire his General Educational Development certificate, then re-entered school to play baseball that spring. He had played international baseball in the junior world championships and the Pan American Games. He had everything he wanted from high school ball and no interest in college.
"Something told me I needed to get out," he said recently in the dugout at Comerica Park, shifting his cap repeatedly on his shaved head as if signaling a runner on base. He had repeated fifth grade as he struggled with dyslexia, he said. All his friends were older than his classmates.
"I needed to take another step forward before I burned myself out playing at one level," he said.
Oakland drafted Bonderman in the first round of the 2001 draft and signed him to a $1.35 million bonus, then traded him last August to Detroit, which kept him in A ball. Last month, he got his first major league victory against the Athletics and their Cy Young award winner Barry Zito. Later, in Detroit, Zito praised Bonderman's pitching but said it "seemed pretty nutty" these days to go to professional baseball straight from high school instead of maturing in college first.
"I would never wish that on anyone," Zito said.
At the end of spring training, Bonderman talked with Al Kaline, the Hall of Fame outfielder who joined the Tigers right out of high school in 1953. Kaline said he had told Bonderman not to be intimidated by the hitters' reputations or their salaries.
The hardest part for him, Kaline said, was the loneliness of living in a hotel or an apartment and playing with guys who were years older. Bonderman alleviates the isolation by living with his fiancee, Amber Cox; they plan to marry in the off-season. Carlos Pena, the Tigers' 24-year-old first baseman, has also mentored him, taking him to the mall, to the bookstore, to dinner.
"The most horrible thing in the world is when you feel you are by yourself," Pena said. "Then you are left with your own demons."
The noise and the traffic and size of major league cities, Bonderman can do without. He would prefer to ride horses than planes and buses. Baseball is the same, though, in the city or the country.
"They turned this into a business, but when you're on the field it's a game," he said. "I'm living up to everything I've always wanted to be."
Cluck, a man of humor and patience, knows that this is a roll of the dice.
"Historically, taking high school pitchers is a very bad investment," Cluck said. Perhaps, he added, Bonderman will be one of the exceptions.

John David Booty
Skipping Off To Southern Cal
As he sat in the stands to watch Evangel Christian Academy's spring game on May 15, John David Booty said, it felt a little weird. He had planned to play in the scrimmage, then complete his senior season next fall before graduating in December and heading to Southern California next spring. Evangel Christian was loaded and would be seeking its ninth state championship since it began playing football in 1989.
In April, however, his father, Johnny, was fired as quarterbacks coach at the school, which is affiliated with the First Assembly of God church in Shreveport. Denny Duron, pastor of the church and chancellor of the school, told The Shreveport Times that the dismissal involved "a ministry matter." Johnny Booty had recently started a home ministry, which the church found threatening, he said in a telephone interview.
John David Booty then decided to forgo his senior year entirely. He will take an English course this summer to complete high school and will report to Southern Cal for the beginning of fall practice. "I felt like I was standing up for my dad and for what I want," he said.
At 18, Booty is apparently charting new football territory by graduating a year early. If anyone seems prepared, he does.
His father was an all-state quarterback at Woodlawn High School in Shreveport, where his predecessors were the future professionals Terry Bradshaw and Joe Ferguson. For 11 straight seasons, Johnny Booty coached all-state quarterbacks at Evangel Christian, including John David's brother, Josh, who is now a backup with the Cleveland Browns, and Brock Berlin, who is expected to start next season at the University of Miami.
At 6-3 and 200 pounds, John David Booty was considered by many the country's top prep quarterback prospect. He was held back in fifth grade to give his body an extra year to develop, a common practice in rabid football regions, and played varsity ball since seventh grade. In the last two seasons, he passed for 87 touchdowns.
"I told him not to make an emotional decision," his father said. "If he had not had older brothers and been exposed to this, I never would have let him do it. Because of his background and his winning two state championships, you want the kid to be challenged and pushed and find a new level."
Some recruiting experts believe Booty's decision will start at least a trickle of football players leaving high school a year early. But Joe Paterno of Penn State, the winningest major-college coach, said he had seen only a couple of players who seemed ready for college football after their junior years in high school.
Pete Carroll, the coach at Southern Cal, said N.C.A.A. rules prohibited him from speaking specifically about Booty until he arrives on campus. In general, Carroll said, these would be very rare cases, driven by families, not by coaches, who presumably prefer athletes to develop another year in high school.
"I don't think it is anything anyone would look for as an advantage," Carroll said.
Dennis Dunn, the coach at Evangel Christian, said he also hoped Booty's case would be an isolated one. Even his players who graduated a semester early had not found much benefit by arriving early in college, he said, noting also that early departures of elite athletes can disrupt the rosters of high school teams in numerous sports.
"We wish John David the best," Dunn said, but added, "I hate to see kids miss out on some of the purest and most exciting times of their lives, when it's not a business, it's still the love of a game."
Booty is already facing a number of questions tinged with skepticism. If he sits on the bench or redshirts next season, will he waste a year that could have been spent playing at Evangel Christian? If he left high school early, will he leave college early?
"If he goes there and stars, everyone will say, 'Great,"' Johnny Booty said. "If he doesn't they will say, 'I told you the kid was too young.' But he's ready academically and emotionally."

Dana Hall
Taking Soccer To a Higher Level
As with many top girls soccer players, Dana Hall was identified early as a star, joined a regional club team and the United States national under-17 team and traveled around the country and to Europe to play. In fact, she did not participate this year for her Kingwood High School team, which was considered lesser competition.
She had planned to graduate from high school next winter, a semester early, but reconsidered because she needed so few courses to complete the state requirements. This year, she took both junior and senior English, and she took correspondence courses this spring in physical education, government and economics. Instead of graduating a semester early, she will now finish high school a year early, and in the top 10 percent of her class, she said.
She will attend Texas in the fall and hopes to compete for a spot as the starting goalkeeper.
"I'm not getting challenged enough in my soccer environment," Hall said, explaining her reason for early graduation. She also said that she had become frustrated over having to go to detention on weekends for having missed 14 days of school this semester while on soccer trips.
"It's not fun chilling with delinquents," she said.
Accelerated graduation in women's soccer extends back at least to 1989, when Mia Hamm enrolled early at the University of North Carolina. Hamm's father was transferred to military duty in Italy, and Anson Dorrance, the North Carolina coach, became her temporary legal guardian. A number of players have since followed Hamm's path by arriving early at North Carolina, and the Tar Heels have become the sport's dominant team, winning 17 collegiate titles.
"Our experience has been very good," Dorrance said. "The kids who have joined us early have gone on to wonderful soccer careers. We've never had academic issues. These were well-adjusted kids."
Not all girls soccer stars have chosen to leave high school early, of course. Heather O'Reilly, the consensus top prep player, stayed through her senior year at East Brunswick High School in New Jersey and will attend North Carolina in the fall. "If it was going to be there a year later, why rush it?" she said.
Inevitably, early arrivals face adjustments, some jarring. Amy Steadman arrived at North Carolina a year and a half ago, leaving high school midway through her junior year. She was from tiny Brevard, N.C., and that first semester she had few friends outside soccer, became deeply upset when she received her first bad grades and, because of her youth, needed her parents' permission to participate in some psychology experiments.
A pair of knee injuries have kept Steadman from playing yet with the Tar Heels, though she is healthy now. The injuries, she said, made her realize that there was more to life than soccer and that happiness and friendships should be factored into the college experience.
Leaving high school midway through her junior year "might be a mistake, socially," Steadman said, adding that while she had no regrets, she would consider finishing her junior year if she could do it over again.
As players increasingly commit to colleges during their junior years, Bill Steffen, the women's soccer coach at Oregon, said some universities were pressuring athletes, giving them as little as two weeks to accept a scholarship offer. Steffen, a former assistant coach at North Carolina, has seen athletes excel after leaving high school early, but he said he generally did not recommend it.
The state of Texas, though, encourages early graduation, offering $1,000 scholarships to students as incentive, said Belinda Zoet, an assistant principal at Kingwood High School. While many students can benefit from completing four years of high school, she said, Hall seemed prepared for accelerated graduation.
"She's very mature, very goal-oriented," Zoet said.
Instead of participating in graduation on May 31, Hall will play in a soccer tournament.
"At graduation, you walk 100 feet across the stage and have one minute of fame," Hall said. "It's a lot more fun to play soccer."

HEADLINE: Sports of The Times; 
Fan in Charge, if Only for a Day

BYLINE:  By GEORGE VECSEY 

 BODY:
WHEN Roger Farina landed his Army Chinook helicopter in Iraq during the recent conflict, he stepped out wearing the orange jacket of the Islanders, the team of his childhood. 
 For that example of extreme hockey loyalty, Farina was given the Seventh Man Award by the National Hockey League on Tuesday, just before the first game of the Stanley Cup finals. 
 His first proposal as commissioner for a day? Disbanding a certain blue-clad franchise based in Manhattan.
There are a lot of fanatics like Farina in a swath across North America, and this is their time of year. They remember televised Saturday night games in Canada, victory handshake lines, Stanley Cup parades. And in the days to come, fans in New Jersey and the nouveau frontier of Anaheim will make a pilgrimage to see the Stanley Cup glittering in public view. 
Roger Farina, a 36-year-old chief warrant officer, Grade 3, has shown his commitment to the Islanders by using two of his boyhood heroes for his daughters' middle names. There is Abigail Nystrom Farina, almost 3, and there is Allison Gillies Farina, almost 1. 
How did Farina accomplish naming two girls after stalwarts from the glorious past? 
"Because I'm in the military, all the benefits are in my name," he explained the other day. "My wife was in the delivery room and she was saying, 'No, no, no,' but I did it anyway. No, actually, she's Canadian. She's a good sport."
Jennifer Farina, from Ottawa, can understand her husband's love for this sport. He has not lived on Long Island for a number of years, but he has transported sticks, jerseys, photos and other Islanders memorabilia to his posts in Asia, Europe and the Middle East. He finds Islanders games on the Internet at all hours of the night and day, but his rule is that they must be live. Taped doesn't count. 
The earliest games Farina can remember were on television at his grandmother's house, where he watched with his older cousin Larry Wolfe and his best friend, Pat Tracey. He can remember where he was when the Islanders won their first of four Stanley Cups in 1980 on an overtime goal by Bobby Nystrom.
"I was outside, mowing the lawn, thanks to my father," Farina said. "You could hear everybody in the neighborhood beeping. Two years later, when they had a victory parade, my friend and I rode our bicycles down to Hempstead Turnpike, which we were not supposed to do."
He joined the Army after high school, and in 1991 he was flying a Cobra helicopter during the Desert Storm combat, "where I got most of my war stories," he said. Farina said he helped capture 500 prisoners of war, but he doesn't go into detail. 
While posted in Europe, he sought out international tournaments and looked up any hockey people from North America. A year ago in the Czech Republic he spotted Nystrom, who works with the Islanders.
"I showed him my daughter's birth certificate, and he started inching away from me," Farina said. "But after awhile he relaxed, and we went shopping together." 
Last year Farina met Clark Gillies, the strapping wing who had just been voted into the Hockey Hall of Fame. When Gillies heard about the second daughter's middle name, he sent her a tiny Islanders jersey, having autographed it. 
The zeal of this pilot, serving his country in a war zone, won him the new N.H.L. Seventh Man Award. But Roger Farina did not merely show up to shake hands. On Tuesday, as commissioner for a day, he attended meetings and presented his concerns about hockey rules, traditions and economics. 
"It was more interactive than I would have thought," said Gary Bettman, the other commissioner. "He would say, 'I know I don't have the full story, but that's me, as a fan.' It was great to see somebody fighting for his country who says hockey is his passion."
One of Farina's proposals is to go back to the old personal names (Adams, Smythe, etc.) for the league's divisions rather than the recent use of generic geographic names. 
"There are two new divisions, so I would name one after Willie O'Ree," he said, referring to the first black player in the league, now a living icon. "And the other one I'd name after Al Arbour, the greatest coach in league history." Arbour, of course, coached the Islanders in their four Stanley Cup seasons.
At lunch, Farina got to sit near his old pal Nystrom and another former Islander, Jean Potvin. Later, he attended a news conference with Bettman and was proud to notice many of his concerns being raised by the hockey writers. 
When it was time to drop the ceremonial first puck before the Mighty Ducks-Devils opener, Farina wore a gift jersey, but then he lifted it to show his Army desert uniform. "The crowd went nuts," he said. 
Now Farina will fly back to duty in Iraq. "My 15 minutes of fame are over," he said. Bettman tried to tell him he was commissioner of all the teams, but Farina has put in a personal order for another Stanley Cup finals on Long Island, soon.

HEADLINE: Paid Notice: Deaths; 
GRANNON, , CHARLES L.

 BODY:
 GRANNON -- Charles L. Charles ("Chuck") Lee Grannon a former partner of Goldman, Sachs & Co., New York, NY, and resident of Boca Raton, FL, formerly of Ridgewood, NJ died March 7, 2003 at age 87. Grannon spent his entire career at Goldman Sachs & Co., joining the firm in 1945, he became a partner in 1959, and a limited partner in 1982. In 1972 he was Chairman of the Corporate Finance Committee of the SIA, a Financial Industry oversight group. Grannon served twice in the Navy, first during World War II from May 1942 until October 1945. During that time he served on Admiral Nimitz's staff in the South Pacific. He was called back in 1951 for the Korean War and served in Washington, DC. He became a Lieutenant Commander in 1951 and was discharged in 1952. He served as a director of Federal - Mogul Corporation from 1968 until 1986. He was also a life trustee of DePauw University in Greencastle, IN, and a lifetime honorary director and past Vice Chairman of the University of Iowa Foundation. Recently, the Valley Hospital Foundation of Ridgewood, NJ elected Grannon, a past president, as an Honorary Life Trustee. He has also served on the boards of several other charitable organizations including: President of the Center for Group Counseling in Boca Raton, FL; Trustee at the West Side Presbyterian Church in Ridgewood, NJ; President of the philanthropic Shamrock Society of Boca Raton, FL. He has also been active with the YMCA of Ridgewood, NJ as a Trustee for over twenty years. 
Through the generosity of Charles and Alice Fay, endowed scholarship programs in business have been established at both DePauw University and the University of Iowa. He was born on December 2, 1915 in Lafayette, IN, the son of Charles D. Grannon and Verena Ruth Benedict Grannon. He received a B.S. from Purdue University, Lafayette, IN in 1939. In 1941 he received an M.A. in Economics from the University of Iowa and married Alice Fay Conard. They were married until her death in December 1995. In 1988, both Charles and Alice Fay were honored by the University of Iowa with a joint Distinguished Alumni Service Award. He graduated from Jefferson High School, Lafayette, IN in 1934. He is survived by his four sons; Michael L. (Lee) of New Canaan, CT; Craig C. (Linda) of Burr Ridge IL; Charles P. (Sandi) of Bloomington, IN; Mark W. (Gai) of New York City, and two grandchildren, Emily and Christopher. A memorial service will be held Saturday, May 31st at 9:30am at C.C. Van Emburgh Funeral Home, 306 East Ridgewood Ave at Maple Avenue in Ridgewood, NJ. >>AD#

HEADLINE: Retail Sales Pace Picks Up, Hinting of Broader Rebound 

BYLINE:  By DAVID LEONHARDT

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 28 

 BODY:
The first signs of a postwar pickup in consumer spending are emerging, reducing the chances that the Federal Reserve will lower interest rates when it meets next month.
 Retail sales at chain stores rose last week at a faster pace than they had for most of the previous month, according to two surveys released today. In one survey, conducted by the Bank of Tokyo-Mitsubishi, sales increased 3.1 percent compared with the week a year earlier, the biggest gain since January. 
 The chain-store numbers are among a handful of indicators receiving close attention from policy makers and investors because the data cover very recent economic activity. The indicators are more volatile than the best-known economic reports, like those on employment and retail sales. But the indicators reflect decisions that consumers and business executives have made since the war with Iraq ended, and they may now offer more insight into the economy's direction, economists say. 
Appearing before Congress last week, Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, said that a series of disappointing reports over the previous month did not send a clear message about the economy because they still reflected concerns about the war. "Many more weeks of data will be needed to confidently discern the underlying trends," Mr. Greenspan said.
Stocks rose again today, and the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index closed at 953.22, its highest level since Aug. 22. 
Investors have also become more skeptical that the Fed will cut its benchmark short-term interest rate when it meets on June 24 and 25, judging from the price of a futures contract based on the rate.
The contract's price suggests that investors think a quarter-point reduction in the rate remains likely. Most forecasters, however, have said that the Fed officials will probably cut the rate either by a half-point, in a sign of their resolve to get the economy moving, or not at all, in a show of confidence. The odds of a half-point reduction are roughly one in three, based on the contract's closing price today, down from about one in two early last week.
The Fed's benchmark rate, which sets the interest rate on many credit-card, automobile and business loans and indirectly affects mortgage rates, is 1.25 percent, its lowest level since 1961. The Fed has cut the rate 12 times since the start of 2001 in an effort to encourage spending and end the lingering economic slowdown.
Over the next week, a series of other reports covering recent activity are likely to help economists determine whether the jump in retail sales is real and economic growth is indeed on the verge of accelerating, as most forecasters predict.
On Thursday, the Labor Department will announce the number of people who filed for unemployment insurance last week. On Friday, the Fed will release its weekly data on the amount of money businesses are borrowing. Next week, car manufacturers will announce their May sales, and an organization of corporate purchasing managers will release surveys about business activity during May.
The retail numbers released today offered tentative evidence that consumers have become more willing to spend money than they were during the first few months of this year, when sales were lower in some weeks than they were in 2002.
The Bank of Tokyo's index of chain-store sales remained flat during February and March before rising slowly in early April, compared to a year earlier, and last week's increase was only the second one above 3 percent this year. The other survey, the Instinet Research Redbook Average, provided a somewhat less optimistic picture, showing a 1.5 percent increase in sales. Still, that was the biggest increase in three weeks.
"Slowly, in a ragged fashion, the industry is showing better performance," said Michael P. Niemira, a senior economist at the Bank of Tokyo. But, he added, "it's not so powerful that everyone is happy."
At Target, the discount chain, sales rebounded last week after disappointing managers early this month, and May sales now appear likely to exceed the company's forecast, it said. Gains were biggest for pharmacy products, baby goods and children's apparel.
Wal-Mart Stores's sales projection has stayed roughly constant throughout the month, the company said.
Surveys of consumer confidence, another indicator offering nearly real-time glimpses of the economy, have also suggested reasons for some optimism. Although many analysts question whether the overall confidence numbers say much about the economy's direction, they say the individual questions within surveys often forecast changes in spending.
One closely followed measure -- consumers' near-term expectations for the economy -- rose this month, the University of Michigan reported on Tuesday. A second common harbinger of turning points is the percentage of Americans saying they expect the next five years to bring periods of widespread unemployment, and that declined to 36 percent in May, from 51 percent in March, the University of Michigan said.
"This is almost a repeat of what we saw at the end of the first gulf war," said Richard T. Curtin, director of surveys at the University of Michigan, who said he thought the economy was improving but still faced problems.
"What we have is the first surge in confidence," Mr. Curtin said. "I think we will see some slide. The question is how much."
Speaking today in Las Vegas, Robert T. Parry, president of the San Francisco Fed, also cautioned against economic exuberance, according to a transcript of his remarks. "At this point, it appears that the economy is still mired in the soft patch we hit last fall," he said.
A "modest pickup" in the second half of the year was the most likely outcome, he added.
In a sign that business investment remained weak, the Commerce Department reported today that orders for durable goods -- expensive items like machinery and vehicles -- fell 2.4 percent in April, a larger decline than economists had expected.

HEADLINE: NEWS WATCH: TRAVEL; 
Who Knew? A Shangri-La Within Driving Distance

BYLINE:  By Bob Tedeschi 

 BODY:
Shorter vacations are again in vogue as a weak-kneed economy persuades more Americans to stick close to home. But coming up with a destination that is both nearby and unexplored can be tricky. 
 Enter TripAdvisor and its new Weekend Getaway Guide, which offers suggestions for trips to places that are 25to 300 miles away from a given ZIP code. The guide, which made its debut in late April, is a weekly e-mail newsletter that users sign up for at tripadvisor.com; in addition to suggested weekend trips, it contains links to published information and user reviews on thousands of tourist destinations in the United States and abroad. 
 For a southern Connecticut resident, TripAdvisor recently e-mailed weekend road trip suggestions for Atlantic City, supplying directions, the driving distance, a five-day weather forecast and recommended attractions and hotels, among other things. In case Atlantic City wasn't a good fit, TripAdvisor included links to eight other archived suggestions, among them Newport, R.I., bucolic Lenox, Mass., and, of course, New York.
Given Atlantic City's raison d'etre, some travelers with young children might quickly dismiss the notion of such a trip. But Trip Advisor's recommended reading detailed several interesting attractions for children, along with beach suggestions. Armed with such information, users might consider a spur-of-the-moment trip to be less of a gamble. Bob Tedeschi

HEADLINE: Economic Scene; 
Cash rewards and poverty alone do not explain terrorism.

BYLINE:  By Alan B. Krueger; Alan B. Krueger is the Bendheim professor of economics and public affairs at Princeton University and a co-editor of The Journal of the European Economic Association. E-mail: akrueger@princeton.edu.

 BODY:
"THE passing of Saddam Hussein's regime will deprive terrorist networks of a wealthy patron that pays for terrorist training, and offers rewards to families of suicide bombers," President Bush predicted in a speech to the American Enterprise Institute in February.
 Others in the administration, including Secretary of State Colin L. Powell, Defense Secretary Donald H. Rumsfeld and his deputy, Paul D. Wolfowitz, highlighted that Iraq's widely reported increase in payments to families of Palestinian suicide bombers, to $25,000 from $10,000, in the spring of 2002, encouraged suicide bomb attacks. Regime change, it was argued, would eliminate the incentive for suicide bombings. 
 This month's spate of suicide bombing attacks in the Middle East -- five in Israel, three in Saudi Arabia and five in Morocco -- should put this argument to rest. The number of suicide attacks per week in Israel was higher in the month after the fall of Baghdad than it was, on average, in the 14 months before the invasion. Of course, this is not a controlled experiment; other contributing factors have changed. But it would seem that the financial incentive provided by Iraq's payments has had little impact on the supply of suicide bombers so far. 
Why were the policy makers wrong? 
One possibility is that there are other wealthy patrons and Islamic charities, whose cash substituted for Saddam Hussein's. 
But I suspect the main reason is that most terrorists are not motivated by the prospect of financial gain or the hopelessness of poverty. 
The stereotype that terrorists are driven to extremes by economic deprivation may never have held anywhere, least of all in the Middle East. New research by Claude Berrebi, a graduate student at Princeton, has found that 13 percent of Palestinian suicide bombers are from impoverished families, while about a third of the Palestinian population is in poverty. A remarkable 57 percent of suicide bombers have some education beyond high school, compared with just 15 percent of the population of comparable age.
This evidence corroborates findings for other Middle Eastern and Latin American terrorist groups. There should be little doubt that terrorists are drawn from society's elites, not the dispossessed. 
Yet some stereotypes die hard. In 1958 the political scientist Daniel Lerner argued, "The data obviate the conventional assumption that the extremists are simply the 'have-nots.' "
It is still possible that well-off people in poor countries with oppressive governments are drawn to terrorism. President Bush argued something along these lines in an Op-Ed article in The New York Times on the anniversary of Sept. 11. "Poverty does not transform poor people into terrorists and murderers," he acknowledged. "Yet poverty, corruption and repression are a toxic combination in many societies, leading to weak governments that are unable to enforce order or patrol their borders and are vulnerable to terrorist networks." 
To investigate this possibility, I have analyzed data the State Department collects on significant international terrorist incidents. The home countries of the perpetrators of each event were identified. More terrorists do come from poor countries than rich ones, but this is because poor countries tend to lack civil liberties. 
Once a country's degree of civil liberties is taken into account -- measured by Freedom House, a nonprofit organization that promotes democracy, as the extent to which citizens are free to develop views, institutions and personal autonomy without interference from the state -- income per capita bears no relation to involvement in terrorism. Countries like Saudi Arabia and Bahrain, which have spawned relatively many terrorists, are economically well off yet lacking in civil liberties. Poor countries with a tradition of protecting civil liberties are unlikely to spawn terrorists.
Evidently, the freedom to assemble and protest peacefully without interference from the government goes a long way to providing an alternative to terrorism. 
Apart from the size of a country and the extent of its civil liberties, no factor that I could find -- including the literacy rate, infant mortality rate, terrain, ethnic divisions and religious fractionalization -- could predict whether people from that country were more or less likely to take part in international terrorism.
After observing that "Saddam Hussein has raised the amount going to suicide bombers from $10,000 to $25,000," the comedian Jay Leno joked, "What's next, a health care plan?" 
Mr. Leno may be on to something. Financial incentives usually influence people's actions, but in this case they have a minor effect. The main motivation is deep devotion to a political, social or religious cause. When freedom of expression and other civil liberties are protected, there are nonviolent ways to express this devotion. 
If this is right, then terrorist attacks should increase in a repressive regime whenever the political situation is not heading in a direction the extremists prefer, irrespective of economic incentives. And terrorism and hate crimes seem to be particularly prevalent when countries go through an evolution in which normal law enforcement is disrupted, as in East Germany after the fall of the Berlin Wall. 
The ultimate joke would be if civil liberties are sacrificed in the fight against terrorism, as a lack of civil liberties seems to be a main cause of terrorism around the world. Support for civil liberties should be part of the arsenal in the war against terrorism, both at home and abroad.

HEADLINE: Justices Urge Raises for Federal Colleagues 

BYLINE:  AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 28 

 BODY:
Armed with a report that shows judges leaving for more lucrative jobs, Chief Justice William H. Rehnquist made a rare public plea with three of his Supreme Court colleagues today to urge better pay for federal judges.
 For years Chief Justice Rehnquist has tried to draw attention to the salary issue in reports to Congress. 
 He stepped up the pressure late last year with a visit to the White House to lobby President Bush. Congress is considering a plan to give judges about $25,000 more a year, which would make up for years without any pay increases. 
"We must provide these judges, whom we ask and expect to remain for life, adequate compensation," Chief Justice Rehnquist told reporters at the court in a news conference with Justices Anthony M. Kennedy, David H. Souter and Stephen G. Breyer.
The report, by the American Bar Association and the Federal Bar Association, shows that more judges have left the bench since 1990 than in the previous three decades. Many took private jobs.
The report also found that circuit and district judges' salaries, compared with inflation, had declined more than 23 percent since 1969. 
The buying power of Supreme Court salaries fell 37 percent.
The justices shrugged off a question about their personal financial sacrifices.
"I'm not taking three-month vacations in Europe, and I have not spent thousands and thousands of dollars educating my children," Chief Justice Rehnquist said.
He said that district judges, who earn about $155,000, can struggle to pay bills.
Justice Souter said that in the course of advising a friend who was being considered for a judgeship: "I said, 'Tell me how you are going to educate your kids.' That was the end of the conversation."
Justice Breyer, one of the wealthier members of the court, said that the pay scale should ensure that people of all backgrounds, not just the rich, would agree to become judges.
Justice Kennedy said there were problems in recruiting judges and keeping them from leaving for higher paying private jobs.
News conferences at the Supreme Court are extremely rare, usually reserved for the announcement of a retirement.
The subject of a possible retirement this year did not come up. Chief Justice Rehnquist, who has been on the bench 31 years, is considered the most likely prospect.
Although Supreme Court justices are federal judges, the four were making their plea on behalf of lower court judges.
The president of the American Bar Association, Alfred P. Carlton Jr., thanked the justices for having "a great deal of personal courage to stand up and talk about how much you make."
The report praises the pay raise plan that has passed a Senate committee, although Mr. Carlton said that it would be better if the raises were not linked to a proposal that gives judges the discretion to allow television cameras in their courtrooms.
After a recent cost-of-living increase, appeals court judges earn $164,000 and Supreme Court justices receive about $190,000. The chief justice is paid more than $198,000.

GRAPHIC: Photo: From left, Justices Stephen G. Breyer, David H. Souter and Anthony M. Kennedy and Chief Justice William H. Rehnquist at a news conference at the Supreme Court to urge better pay for federal judges. Congress is considering a plan to give $25,000 more a year, to make up for years without increases. (Stephen Crowley/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Good News From Brazil 

 BODY:
The global economy may not be the happiest of stories these days, but it would be a far more tragic one had Brazil suffered a financial implosion in the past year, as many had feared. If Brazil, Latin America's largest nation, had defaulted on its $250 billion public debt, as neighboring Argentina had done, the consequences would have been catastrophic. The resulting panic would have affected not only Latin America, but all emerging markets. 
 The fact that this nation of 175 million is no longer on the brink of unleashing a global financial crisis -- and is instead helping to lead an emerging-market recovery -- is a tribute to the sound policies pursued by the five-month-old government of its leftist president, Luiz Inacio Lula da Silva, and to a $30 billion rescue package the International Monetary Fund made available to Brazil last September. It is that rare success story in which the I.M.F. averts a disaster, as it was meant to do. In this case, the I.M.F. simply issued a vote of confidence in a country whose economic fundamentals were sound. That helped assuage investors' worries about the prospects of a da Silva presidency. 
 Since taking office in January, President da Silva has pursued policies that have strengthened the nation's financial standing. Brazil's currency and bonds have rallied strongly, easing its debt burden, and rampant inflation is beginning to taper off. The government is also maintaining a budget surplus.
Some of the president's most ardent supporters within his Workers' Party are not thrilled by all this economic orthodoxy, or by what Mr. da Silva himself calls "bitter medicine." The government's tough reform proposals for civil service pensions may cause further friction between the administration and party militants. But the president, a former labor leader, is right in saying he cannot succeed on his ambitious long-term social welfare agenda unless Brazil first gets its economic house in order.
A lot is riding on the outcome of Mr. da Silva's struggle to navigate between short-term reality and his grander aspirations -- not only for Brazil, but also for a growing number of left-of-center Latin leaders who look to him as a role model. Washington also seems to be warming to him. Treasury Secretary John Snow's first foreign trip was to Brazil last month, and it was announced last Thursday that Mr. da Silva would visit the White House next month.
Brazil and the United States, the two most populous nations in the Americas, are negotiating to forge a free trade area in the region by 2005. Success is far from certain. Brazil is a leading critic of indefensible farm subsidies in the United States and in Europe, yet is not keen on fully opening some of its own markets to foreign competition. Both sides need to offer concessions. It will be in Latin America's overall interest for the Bush and da Silva administrations to create a good working relationship as they tackle these difficult issues.

HEADLINE: Caught in the Squeeze 

BYLINE:  By BOB HERBERT; E-mail: bobherb@nytimes.com

 BODY:
One of the things President Bush knows best is when to turn on the klieg lights, and when to keep them off.
 On Tuesday, with no fanfare, he signed a bill increasing the federal debt limit by nearly a trillion dollars. You don't want a lot of coverage when you're mortgaging the future. 
 But yesterday it was high-fives all around as Mr. Bush signed the third-largest tax cut in history at a grand ceremony in the East Room of the White House. 
I suppose if your income is large enough, there is every reason to celebrate. After all, the tax cut could save Dick Cheney $100,000 a year, or more.
But given the economic realities in the U.S. right now, I thought the East Room celebration was in poor taste. The enormous tax-cut package (which is coupled with budget deficits that are lunging toward infinity) is a stunning example of Mr. Bush's indifference to the deepening plight of working people.
The economy has lost more than a half-million jobs already this year, and well over 2 million since payrolls peaked two years ago. More than 8.7 million American men and women are officially counted as unemployed. And that figure is artificially low because it does not count those who have become discouraged and stopped looking for work.
The fallout from the continued hemorrhaging of jobs and the swollen ranks of the unemployed is spreading. The Times had an article two weeks ago about college seniors' putting their dreams on hold because they're graduating into the worst hiring slump in 20 years.
"We definitely picked the wrong time to be graduating from college," said Morgan Bushey, a 21-year-old student at the University of North Carolina. She said she planned to go to France, where she would make about $200 a week teaching English.
The jobs squeeze has other effects. "There's been this notion along the way that if you at least kept your job, you'd be O.K.," said Jared Bernstein, an economist with the Economic Policy Institute in Washington. "But now this persistent unemployment is taking a toll on the wages of those who are still working."
Wages, when adjusted for inflation, are falling for workers across the board. An analysis of government data by Mr. Bernstein and Lawrence Mishel, the institute's president, found that the median weekly paycheck fell 1.4 percent over the past year. All the pay grades above and below the median are also sliding backward. White-collar, blue-collar -- workers in all pay grades are taking a hit. Even wage earners in the highest category have seen their pay slip by 1.4 percent.
"When unemployment got down to 4 percent in the late-1990's, you had broad-based wage growth -- and it was the first time we'd seen that in decades," said Mr. Bernstein. "That's gone."
The president is not calling his tax package the "Windfall for the Wealthy" act, which is what it is. He calls it the "Jobs and Growth" act, which is what it's not.
He would like us to believe that "with tax relief will come more jobs for the American people." But that's what he said in the last round of tax cuts, and the American people are still waiting.
In fact, the wait is becoming interminable for some. More and more Americans are joining the ranks of the long-term unemployed, those who are out of work for six months or more. A joint study by the National Employment Law Project and the Economic Policy Institute called long-term unemployment "the scourge of a declining economy," and noted that it is taking its greatest toll among those who have traditionally felt economically secure.
"The reality," said the study, "is that the long-term unemployed are better educated, older and more likely to be professional workers."
What the economy needs is a real stimulus that will create real jobs, not an irresponsible package of tax cuts that will inflate the portfolios of the very wealthy while starving the government of the money needed to pay for essential services and to maintain a safety net for the nation's most vulnerable citizens.
We are closing schools and libraries in America, and withholding lifesaving drugs and medical treatment from the poor. The middle class is struggling ever harder to make ends meet, and reshaping its dreams of the future.
In Washington, they're celebrating.

HEADLINE: Battle Over Biotechnology Intensifies Trade War 

BYLINE:  By ELIZABETH BECKER with DAVID BARBOZA

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 28 

 BODY:
President Bush said last week that Europe's opposition to genetically altered crops was a threat to efforts to end world hunger.
 But even many critics of Europe's stance say that the president's argument does not stand up and that the dispute needs to be understood for what it is: a multibillion-dollar cross-Atlantic battle over agricultural trade. 
 The disagreement will be played out this week at the meeting in France of the leading industrial countries. It pits European leaders, who say they are worried about the safety of importing genetically altered crops from the United States, against the Bush administration, which insists that Europe's attempts to block the crops are an illegal trade tactic.
The trade dispute heated up after an intense lobbying effort here in Washington, where some of the nation's most powerful interest groups -- farmers, the food industry and giant biotechnology companies -- have been pressing the administration to take on their case at a time of heightened tensions between the United States and Europe. 
Lawyers and lobbyists for some interest groups have descended on the White House and Capitol Hill over the last few weeks to influence policy makers and lawmakers, and in some cases, to simply remind them of the importance of the Farm Belt in the next election.
Some of the biggest agriculture and biotechnology companies have invested billions of dollars over the last decade to develop genetically altered crops. Nearly 100 million acres of farmland in the United States are now planted with genetically altered crops, and agriculture officials say farmers have lost at least $1 billion over the last five years because they have been unable to export some biotechnology crops to Europe.
"We've been very patient with the Europeans, but their use of this ban as a trade barrier sets a precedent for countries around the world," said Mary Kay Thatcher, director of public policy at the American Farm Bureau Federation.
"We rely on export markets for one-third of our crops; this is a nightmare," she added.
Last week, the United States filed the equivalent of a lawsuit at the World Trade Organization, arguing that Europe's effort to block some genetically altered crops violated international trade rules.
At the Group of 8 summit in France this week, the Bush administration is expected to press its case that Europe accept genetically altered crops. But instead of arguing in the name of Monsanto -- the giant of agricultural biotechnology companies -- or American farmers, Mr. Bush and his aides will raise the issue of fighting world hunger.
In a speech last week he accused Europe of hindering the "great cause of ending hunger in Africa" by banning genetically modified crops. Administration officials say that such moves by Europe encourage African nations to reject technology that could save millions of lives.
That has upset European diplomats who are negotiating a compromise on biotechnology.
"It is quite shocking of Mr. Bush to tell us to follow his lead on African aid when the United States gives one of the smallest proportion of its gross domestic product for global development than any other wealthy nation," said a senior diplomat here. "This has not helped us."
Pascal Lamy, the top European trade official, even challenged the notion that Europe has a moratorium, saying that Europe is on the verge of completing new regulations that could open up the Continent to more genetically modified crops. Europe approved the sale of genetically altered soybeans in the 1990's, but then in 1998 Europe instituted a moratorium on approving new biotechnology crops like certain varieties of genetically altered corn. So while soybeans have been largely unaffected by the moratorium, corn exports have been harmed.
Several agriculture experts who want to lift European restrictions said that the problem would not be solved by opening up Europe's market.
"It's quite a stretch to tie the problem of the ban against genetically modified food in Europe to starving children in Africa," said Dan Glickman, who served as secretary of agriculture in the Clinton administration. "It is also a bit provocative to say the Europeans don't care about world hunger."
Scientists also agree.
"In general, that is not the case at all," said Pedro Sanchez, director of tropical agriculture at the Earth Institute at Columbia University.
"The main problems in Africa have to do with soil fertility," he said. "Until you solve the soil problems, it doesn't matter whether you use conventional or genetically modified seeds."
Backers of genetically altered crops say that they have been properly tested and that there is no scientific evidence that they pose a risk to humans or the environment.
Mickey Kantor, the first trade representative for President Clinton and a lawyer whose firm represents Monsanto, says the trade dispute has grown beyond complaints from biotechnology companies.
"It's not just about the industry anymore," he said. "It is a technology that can have a positive effect on world hunger."
If the biotechnology companies had done more for poor countries, that argument might hold, said Peter Pringle, author of the coming book, "Food, Inc.," (Simon & Schuster 2003).
Instead, he writes in his study of biotechnology, "while the industry claimed that their products would save the world from malnutrition, seed companies created only crops that made money for themselves and the wealthier farmers who could afford the premiums."
The current trade debate centers on opposing views about food safety and the need to test a product before it is put on the grocery shelf. How this dispute is resolved could determine the future course of agriculture, according to many agriculture economists.
Genetically altered crops, which have been biologically altered to do things like release their own insecticide, are already planted on more than 140 million acres worldwide, mostly in North and South America.
But consumers and regulators in Europe worry that the crops could pose a threat to humans or the environment.
Five years ago, Europe placed a moratorium on approving biotechnology crops. In preparing to end the moratorium, Europe is planning to impose new rules and regulations to trace crops back to their origin and label all genetically modified products, a move that could make it more difficult for Americans to export their biotechnology crops to Europe.
America's two biggest agricultural exports -- corn and soybeans -- could be greatly threatened by the new regulations to label the product, industry officials say. 
"We think that's the equivalent of putting a skull and crossbones on the packages, saying these things are bad," said Bob Callanan, a spokesman for the American Soybean Association in St. Louis.
American exports of corn to Europe have virtually dried up because corn farmers have widely adopted a form of biotech corn that kills pests.
"We went from about a 1.5 million metric ton market in 1998 to 23,000 metric tons, so it's pretty much been obliterated," said Hayden Milberg, director of public policy at the National Corn Growers Association, which is based in St. Louis.
The corn industry estimates that it has lost more than $1 billion since the moratorium. 
Some farmers are questioning the administration's strategy for opening the European market.
Harvey Joe Sanner, the director of the Soybean Producers of America, said Europe was the largest export customer for soybeans last year and he criticized some of the stronger remarks made by Robert B. Zoellick, the United States trade representative, in the current dispute.
"We are very concerned with the harsh rhetoric of late by Mr. Zoellick," he said in a statement today. "I am wondering how brilliant it is for a key government official, who should be promoting sales of U.S. soybeans, to use such derogatory terms in describing our largest single buyer."

HEADLINE: Bush Visit Will Lift Poland to Status of Special Friend 

BYLINE:  By RICHARD BERNSTEIN

DATELINE: WARSAW, May 28 

 BODY:
President Bush is scheduled to stop in Poland later this week to spend a few hours on Friday and Saturday in the southern city of Krakow, and the point of his visit is obvious: to thank this country for supporting American policy in Iraq when other, bigger members of the Atlantic alliance campaigned vociferously against it.
 Mr. Bush's short stopover, during which he will visit the Auschwitz Concentration Camp Museum near Krakow, signals that Poland, in the enthusiastic eyes of Washington, has become an important ally, even a special friend -- to be distinguished from obstructionist allies in "old Europe," notably France and Germany. 
It is a strange and even improbable role for a country like Poland, whose tragic and tormented history kept it in the shadows for centuries. 
In Poland itself, the American wish for the European center of gravity to shift eastward and for Poland to play a large role is both gratifying and a bit awkward. Years ago, Poland's post-Communist leadership decided to find ways of enhancing its security by allying itself as closely as possible with the United States, but the potential costs to ties with closer nations in Europe were little weighed.
"Not all the consequences are positive, because of the fact that some European countries, especially France and Germany, adopted different policies on Iraq," Poland's foreign minister, Wlodzimierz Cimoszewicz, said in an interview. "There were controversies, and, unfortunately, there is still something in the air, especially with France."
But, Mr. Cimoszewicz continued, "It's also an echo of the fact that our Western European partners and friends have to realize and accept that Poland is a serious partner, and should be respected. Its arguments should be listened to."
"We have 40 million people with good prospects," Mr. Cimoszewicz said. "We are the biggest state among those joining the E.U., and we have to play an important role."
What role exactly that will be -- assuming enough Poles vote to join the European Union in a June 8 referendum -- is the question being asked these days.
There are people here and elsewhere in Europe who believe that Poland will be swallowed whole by its more influential European partners.
Elsewhere in Europe, there have been undisguised expressions of astonishment and even contempt at Poland's newfound muscularity, a sense that the country is getting too big for its breeches. In remarks much noted here, the phrase "Trojan donkey" appeared in the German press to describe a Poland that was doing America's bidding in Europe, rather than working to create a counterbalance to American power.
And yet alongside any awkwardness Poland may feel at being caught between Europe and the United States goes unaccustomed security, and pride at standing on the same battlefields as the Americans, particularly in Iraq, where Poland is to send 1,500 soldiers to keep peace and will command one of four occupation zones.
Poland might not be able to pull Europe into strategic alignment with the United States, but influential Poles feel that it is nonetheless capable of exerting more influence than other European countries, accustomed to a weak and poor Poland, expect.
"Do we think we're a great world power? No," said Wanda Rapaczynski, the chief executive officer of Agora, a large publisher of newspapers and magazines. "Is this an unexpected element that tickles our fantasy? Yes."
"Of course, we have to limit our ambitions and make them proportionate to our actual potential," Mr. Cimoszewicz said. "But on the other hand, we should not underestimate our ability and potential. We have to find a real place for our country."
Krzysztof Bobinski , an editor and commentator, put it this way: "The majority view is that America will be the ultimate guarantor of Poland's security, and that the way to assure that is for Poland to go to Iraq and, perhaps, other places even farther afield. The alternative view would be that we have a long road ahead of us, and that the E.U. gives us a better chance in the long run."
By most comparisons, Poland does seem very small and backward compared to the bigger members of the European Union. Germany's annual trade with the United States, roughly $50 billion a year, is 50 times that of Poland. Germany's per capita income is five times that of Poland. France is Poland's largest source of foreign investment, and Germany is its largest market.
Poland's Solidarity workers' movement was a great factor in precipitating the end of the cold war, and the country has transformed both its economy and political institutions -- a strategic turn toward democracy and the American model developed in part to counter the weight of historic rival Russia looming to the east. But Poland has now had three bad economic years in a row, unemployment is running at close to 20 percent, and its government has been badly tainted by recent corruption scandals, giving rise to a palpable public cynicism.
Warsaw, the capital, is the kind of place where there are impressive goods to buy in the shops and crowds of people moving briskly, clearly with things to do. New international-class hotels have sprouted, and yet the dominant feature on the skyline remains the old Stalinist Palace of Culture built during the Communist period.
"Poland is not a dynamic economy," Mr. Bobinski said.
Two-thirds of Polish farmers produce only what they consume themselves, and nothing for the market, according to Piotr Stasinski, deputy editor in chief of the daily Gazeta Wyborcza. There are five times as many people, proportionately speaking, in agriculture in Poland than in Western Europe. The news magazine Poland Monthly now has a cover story called "The Other Poland," which reports on widespread hunger in rural areas.
But if a certain backwardness persists, Poland still represents half of the total population and half the gross domestic product of the 10 former Communist countries due tojoin the European Union next year.
Since joining NATO, it has also moved to enhance its air power and in so doing accentuate ties with the United States -- in three years, it will take delivery of 48 F-16 planes that it has bought from the United States, defying European pressure to buy Mirage 2000 fighters.
"This is a big country with a sense of history," Christopher Hill, the American ambassador, said in an interview. "It's not just a former grimy Communist country trying to privatize its textile mills. They're not going to be agenda takers; they're going to be agenda setters."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Poland backed the Iraq war and emerged as a key American ally. Gen. Andrzej Tyszkiewicz of Poland will command a force in central Iraq. (Associated Press)
