HEADLINE: Bush Signs Tax Cut Bill, Dismissing All Criticism 

BYLINE:  By RICHARD W. STEVENSON

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 28 

 BODY:
President Bush signed into law today the third tax cut in three years, dismissing criticism of the legislation's cost, fairness and effectiveness, while casting the measure as "essential action to strengthen the American economy." 
 Despite widespread disagreement among economists about the bill's potency as economic medicine in the short run and its implications for the national debt in the long run, Mr. Bush hailed the legislation as a job-creating measure that would also put much-needed cash in the hands of working people. 
 "By ensuring that Americans have more to spend, to save and to invest, this legislation is adding fuel to an economic recovery," Mr. Bush said in a signing ceremony in the East Room of the White House. 
Having aggressively sold the tax cut as a cure-all for an economy that has not been growing fast enough to prevent a steady loss of jobs or encourage a recovery in the stock market, Mr. Bush has much riding politically on whether it delivers on its promise -- or is perceived to do so, given forecasts that the economy is recovering gradually anyway.
After studying the final version of the legislation for several days, economists rendered judgments that reflected both analytical divisions over the ways in which tax cuts affect the behaviors of consumers, investors and businesses, and ideological rifts over the use of tax policy to shape the size and role of government.
"This is the most pro-growth tax cut we've seen since 1981, going back to the first Reagan tax cut," said Brian S. Wesbury, an economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens & Thompson, an investment firm in Chicago. Mr. Wesbury, a proponent of tax-cutting "supply side" economics, said the package would easily provide enough punch to meet the White House's promise of creating a million jobs by the end of next year.
But Lawrence Mishel, president of the Economic Policy Institute, a liberal-leaning research organization in Washington, said the administration's own economic forecast, published in the "Economic Report of the President" in February, projected that the economy would create 4.4 million jobs in the next two years even without the stimulus of a new tax cut. If the tax cut is to be judged by its success in creating jobs, Mr. Mishel said, the standard should be whether it produces a million or more new jobs above the 4.4 million already forecast. The economy has lost more than two million jobs since Mr. Bush became president, measured by the number of non-farm employees, including a loss of several hundred thousand jobs this year alone. Few economists foresee the economy generating more than five million new jobs in the next 18 months, before Election Day 2004.
"The administration's policy was never really about job creation," Mr. Mishel said. "It was about shifting the burden of taxation onto wage earners and off people who have capital income, and about shrinking the government."
A study by the Heritage Foundation, a conservative research group, projected that the number of jobs will increase by 3.2 million by the end of next year, with 500,000 of those a result of the tax cut.
Mark M. Zandi, chief economist of Economy.com, a forecasting and consulting firm, projected that the tax cut would generate about 130,000 jobs this year and an additional 350,000 next year. But over the next decade, he said, the tax cut would actually cost jobs, in part because the resulting rise in budget deficits would push interest rates higher.
Many private economists are projecting that the deficit will reach at least $400 billion this year and next year, up from $158 billion last year, and that deficits are likely to persist for the foreseeable future. Although Mr. Bush quietly signed a $984 billion increase in the national debt limit on Tuesday, raising the limit to $7.4 trillion, Treasury department officials said the limit wouldmost likely have to be raised again between next April and October, in the heat of the presidential campaign.
"The tax cut will provide a small but much-needed near-term boost to the struggling economy," Mr. Zandi said. In the long run, he said, "Given the tax cutting we've done to date and the obvious spending requirements we have with defense and homeland security, we're going to have a very serious fiscal problem."
Economists also raised questions about whether the bill would do much to help stock prices. Ari Fleischer, the White House spokesman, said the White House continued to believe that the reductions in taxes on capital gains and dividend income would "restore more confidence in markets." But Mr. Zandi concluded that the provisions would lift stock prices by no more than 2.5 percent if they are allowed to expire, as they would in 2008 under the law signed by Mr. Bush, and by no more than 5 percent if the provisions are made permanent, as Republicans hope to do.
Mr. Bush dismissed the criticism in general terms. With this "bold legislation, we're sending a clear message to the doubters that Washington can respond," Mr. Bush said, likening the tax cut to the nation's response to terrorism, two wars and a corporate scandal.
The bill will reduce income tax rates, increase the child credit, cut the rates on capital gains and dividends, provide tax breaks for small businesses and send $20 billion to state governments facing budget crises. It is valued at $350 billion over 10 years, although if Republicans succeed in their efforts to make permanent provisions in the bill that would otherwise expire, the price tag would rise to more than $800 billion.
The White House said workers would begin to benefit from the cut as soon as next month, as employers reduce the amount of money they withhold from paychecks to adjust for the reduction in income tax rates. Mr. Bush said 25 million families will receive checks in July to reflect the increase in the child credit.
The measure, which won approval on Capitol Hill last week, was the latest manifestation of Mr. Bush's success in pushing his tax-cutting agenda through Congress. He did so this time despite tepid public support in polls for tax cuts as an economic remedy, a closely divided Senate and vociferous objections from most Democrats, who said it favored the wealthy over lower-income people and would leave the nation deeply in debt as it faced the costs of providing retirement and health benefits to an aging population.
With the presidential campaign already heating up, this latest tax cut is sure to be subject to especially intense political scrutiny. Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, a presidential candidate who has urged the repeal of Mr. Bush's entire 2001 tax cut, called the legislation signed into law today "unfair, unaffordable and ineffective."
Senator Joseph I. Lieberman of Connecticut, another of the party's nine declared candidates for the White House, said Mr. Bush's policies were "giving the most to those who need it least, piling more debt on the backs of our children and robbing Social Security and Medicare to pay for it."
But the focus on tax cuts could further expose the fissures within the Democratic Party over economic policy, diluting, at least until the party has a presidential nominee to rally around, the power of the opposition argument against Mr. Bush. The Democratic presidential candidates are divided over whether all of Mr. Bush's tax cuts should be repealed or allowed to stand. 
Two Democrats in the Senate, Zell Miller of Georgia and Ben Nelson of Nebraska, gave Mr. Bush his winning margin on the tax cut he signed today, and 12 Democrats in the Senate voted for Mr. Bush's 2001 tax cut.

CORRECTION-DATE: May 30, 2003

A chart yesterday with an article about President Bush's signing of tax-cut legislation misstated the estimated revenue loss if the cuts were to be extended rather than allowed to expire, as the legislation is written, over the next several years. The estimate is $807 billion through 2013, not $870 billion.

HEADLINE: Details Set for Meeting With Bush in Mideast 

BYLINE:  By ELISABETH BUMILLER

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 28 

 BODY:
President Bush will hold a three-way meeting in Jordan next week with Prime Minister Ariel Sharon of Israel and Mahmoud Abbas, the new Palestinian prime minister, White House officials said today.
 The announcement of the meeting in the Red Sea port city of Aqaba formalized Mr. Bush's deepening personal involvement in trying to resolve the Middle East conflict. It is part of an initiative that is full of potential chances for failure. But administration officials said they saw little choice but for Mr. Bush to lend his prestige and credibility to the effort after the Iraq war. 
 They also said they hoped that both the Israelis and Palestinians would take some concrete steps toward peace before the three leaders meet on Wednesday.
Mr. Bush is to go on Tuesday to the resort town of Sharm el Sheik in Egypt, where he will meet with a number of Arab leaders, including Mr. Abbas. Mr. Sharon will not be included in that meeting, an administration official said, because Crown Prince Abdullah of Saudi Arabia objected. 
Condoleezza Rice, the national security adviser, said in a briefing to reporters today that Mr. Sharon would not be at the meeting of Arab leaders because "the format that we decided on was one that we thought worked best, and this format will work best."
Yasir Arafat, the longtime Palestinian leader, is to be excluded from the Sharm el Sheik gathering at the insistence of White House officials, who have been trying for months to marginalize him. Nonetheless, Arab diplomats said today that they believed that no agreement on the Middle East's future could be reached without the approval of Mr. Arafat.
White House officials said the meeting in Aqaba would take place with the caveat of "conditions permitting," apparently meaning that it could be called off if there was another round of violence or suicide bombings. But they also said that conditions now were better than they had been in many months for pushing both parties toward the idea of two nations, Israel and Palestine, existing side by side in the Middle East.
"The president just believes that this is a good time to sit down, face to face, eye to eye, with the leaders who have responsibility for trying to bring about that peace," Ms. Rice said.
The meeting in Aqaba became possible last weekend, when Mr. Sharon persuaded the Israeli government to endorse the American-backed peace plan, known as the road map, and to officially accept a Palestinian claim to statehood. But many details remain to be worked out. White House officials made it clear that the meeting in Aqaba would be a very early first step.
"This is going to be a long process, and it is going to have ups and downs, as it has always had," Ms. Rice said.
White House officials have been working on a meeting of the three leaders since Mr. Sharon canceled a May 20 visit to Washington because of a suicide bombing. With the White House visit off, administration officials began exploring the idea of a meeting of Mr. Bush, Mr. Sharon and Mr. Abbas in the Middle East at the end of a trip by Mr. Bush to an economic summit meeting in Evian, France.
Administration officials said the original location for a three-way meeting was Sharm el Sheik, the same spot President Bill Clinton visited on several occasions to try to bring the Israelis and Palestinians together. In October 2000, Mr. Clinton brokered a truce between Mr. Arafat and Ehud Barak, the Israeli prime minister at the time. But this time, administration officials said, Egypt was reluctant to be the host of a meeting that would include Mr. Sharon.
As a result, the administration officials came up with the solution of the two meetings, one with the Arab leaders in Sharm el Sheik -- which is also to include President Hosni Mubarak of Egypt and King Abdullah of Jordan -- and the other the three-way meeting in Aqaba.
The meetings in Jordan and Egypt will come at the end of a six-day trip by Mr. Bush to Europe and the Middle East. The president is to leave this Friday for Poland, where he will tour the sites of the concentration camps of Auschwitz and Birkenau and deliver a speech about Europe and the trans-Atlantic alliance in Krakow.
On Saturday, Mr. Bush will meet with President Vladimir V. Putin of Russia in St. Petersburg for the city's 300th-anniversary celebration. He will then travel to a meeting of the leading industrialized democracies, known as the Group of 8, in Evian, France. There Mr. Bush will have his first face-to-face meeting since the war in Iraq with President Jacques Chirac of France. Mr. Bush will also meet with Hu Jintao, the new president of China.
From Evian, Mr. Bush will travel to the Middle East. After his meetings in Egypt and Jordan, he will make a final stop to see American troops at the United States Central Command forward headquarters in Doha, Qatar, where he will meet with the Bush administration's new civilian administrator for Iraq, L. Paul Bremer III, and Gen. Tommy Franks, the commander of allied forces in Iraq.
White House officials said there were no plans at this time for Mr. Bush to go to Iraq, where security and the delivery of basic services are unstable.

HEADLINE: Tax Law Omits $400 Child Credit for Millions 

BYLINE:  By DAVID FIRESTONE

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 28 

 BODY:
A last-minute revision by House and Senate leaders in the tax bill that President Bush signed today will prevent millions of minimum-wage families from receiving the increased child credit that is in the measure, say Congressional officials and outside groups.
 Most taxpayers will receive a $400-a-child check in the mail this summer as a result of the law, which raises the child tax credit, to $1,000 from $600. It had been clear from the beginning that the wealthiest families would not receive the credit, which is intended to phase out at high incomes. 
 But after studying the bill approved on Friday, liberal and child advocacy groups discovered that a different group of families would also not benefit from the $400 increase -- families who make just above the minimum wage. 
Because of the formula for calculating the credit, most families with incomes from $10,500 to $26,625 will not benefit. The Center on Budget and Policy Priorities, a liberal group, says those families include 11.9 million children, or one of every sixchildren under 17.
"I don't know why they would cut that out of the bill," said Senator Blanche Lincoln, the Arkansas Democrat who persuaded the full Senate to send the credit to many more low income families before the provision was dropped in conference. "These are the people who need it the most and who will spend it the most. These are the people who buy the blue jeans and the detergent and who will stimulate the economy with their spending."
Ms. Lincoln noted that nearly half of all taxpayers in her state had adjusted gross incomes that were less than $20,000.
Families with incomes lower than $10,500 will also not receive the refund checks. But under the 2001 tax revision, they would not have been eligible for either the $600 or the $1,000 credits because they do not pay federal taxes. Proposals to give them the credits failed on the House and Senate floors on party-line votes.
The Senate provision that did pass was intended to help those families making $10,500 to $26,625 who do pay federal taxes and could have taken all or part of the $600 credit. The provision, which would have cost $3.5 billion, would have allowed those families to receive some or all of the extra $400 in the new law.
Most families with children who make about $30,000 or less are also eligible for the earned income credit, which the law does not not change. In addition, the law has a few other benefits for low income earners, like expanding the lowest tax bracket and a temporary reduction in the penalty on two-income couples.
Several centrist senators worked hard to make the child credit fully refundable for all low income families, and the full Senate voted this month to include a provision that would have included the minimum-wage families. But the provision was dropped in the House-Senate conference, where tax writers spent days trying to cram many tax cuts -- most prominently, cuts in the taxes on stock dividends and capital gains -- into a bill that the Senate said could not be larger than $350 billion.
House Republicans, who acknowledged the gap on the child credit, blamed the Senate for insisting on its $350 billion cap, saying the low-income families could have been covered had the Senate been more flexible. 
A spokeswoman for the Republicans on the House Ways and Means Committee, Christin Tinsworth, noted that the provision was included in an agreement reached last week by Representative Bill Thomas, Republican of California, the committee chairman, and Senator Charles E. Grassley, Republican of Iowa, chairman of the Senate Finance Committee.
That agreement would have cost $380 billion, but it fell apart when an important swing senator, George V. Voinovich, Republican of Ohio, said he could not approve any bill that exceeded $350 billion. To satisfy him and the Senate, Ms. Tinsworth said, the child credit provision was dropped, along with other costs.
"The Senate preferred to have $20 billion in state aid," she said. "But when we had to squeeze it all to $350 billion, they weren't talking about the child credits. This bill does a lot to help people who need help. But its primary purpose was to generate jobs. Apparently, whatever we do is not going to be enough for some segments of the population."
But Democrats and children's advocacy groups said the Republican demand for large cuts in the dividend tax, which they said benefits primarily wealthy taxpayers, pushed away the credit from low income families.
"If we were going to have a tax cut to give $1,000 to all these other kids, there's no reason not to include these kids, too," said David Harris, president of the Children's Research and Education Institute. "Their families are working and playing by the rules and are left out, though it would not have cost too much to include them."
A spokeswoman for Mr. Voinovich said the senator would have been happy to extend the child credits, but believed that the entire package should not pass $350 billion. The tax writers were free to reduce the dividend tax cut, noted the spokeswoman, Marcie Ridgway.
The gap in the number of families who receive the child credit occurs because of how the formula was arranged in 2001. Congress decided then to give refunds of the credit to low income families, but just to a maximum of 10 percent of the amount they made over $10,000, or a refund of $600, whichever was lower. The $10,000 amount was indexed to inflation and is now $10,500.
When the credit was raised to $1,000, many families could not qualify for the extra amount, because the 10 percent maximum still limited them. Ms. Lincoln proposed raising the formula to 15 percent, which would have covered the increase in the credit for most of those families. Her proposal made it through the Senate Finance Committee, but later she voted against the full cut. 
Because her vote and those of other supporters were not necessary for final passage, Republicans knew they could drop the provision without hurting the bill's chances in the Senate.
"I guess this shows us what our priorities are," Ms. Lincoln said. "I think this tax bill is very irresponsible in the way it treats families."

CORRECTION-DATE: June 5, 2003

 CORRECTION:
A front-page article last Thursday about a revision in the new tax law that will prevent millions of minimum-wage families from receiving a $400 child credit misstated the tax situation of families earning less then $10,500. While they are exempt from federal income tax, many do pay Social Security and Medicare taxes.

HEADLINE: HAVENS; 
Weekender Millbrook, N.Y.

BYLINE:  By ELLEN MAGUIRE 

 BODY:
WHEN Harvard dismissed Timothy Leary, the psychology professor turned psychedelic drug guru, in 1963, he dropped out, at the invitation of a wealthy benefactor, to Millbrook. This polished enclave of vast estates and hundred-acre horse farms set in the lush hills of the eastern Hudson River Valley became the unlikely home of Leary's Castalia Institute, where he conducted controversial experiments with LSD. (It is also where he was arrested by G. Gordon Liddy, then with the Dutchess County Sheriff's Department, in one of several brushes with the law.)
 The Castalia Institute no longer exists and these days Millbrook has a different kind of research center, the Institute of Ecosystem Studies, with a director, Gene Liken, who studies a different kind of acid: acid rain. Many weekend residents are patrons. "It's an oasis," said Ronald S. Gross, a corporate lawyer with a Ph.D. in botany who has an apartment in Manhattan and a house in Millbrook. "We listen to top scientists lecture on significant issues like Lyme disease." 
 Otherwise, Millbrook hasn't changed much. Greek Revival homes sheltered by mature oaks and sugar maples, rare breeds of cattle grazing the open fields and a 130-acre vineyard heighten the sense of centuries-old gentility. Weekend residents shoot pheasant and quail, perfect their fly-fishing casts and visit antiques shops. 
Luxury cars jockey for position outside Mabbettsville Market, an upscale grocery. Inside, women in riding boots and men in spotless barn jackets queue up for espresso. Pockets remain deep, and at least five philanthropic foundations operate in Millbrook.
Some local residents, however, are struggling because of the weakened economy; the Northeast Dutchess Fund of the Berkshire Taconic Community Foundation was started in 2002 with $100,000 from a publicity-shy second-home owner to help residents who lack phones, plumbing, kitchens or health insurance. 
Residents of the village of Millbrook and the town of Washington, of which it is a part, have Millbrook mailing addresses, and most people refer to the entire area as Millbrook. "It's a desirable address," Mayor Don Briggs said with a laugh. "Sometimes we catch residents of other towns slipping over here to get a post office box."

The Scene
Ric Ocasek of the Cars, Mary Tyler Moore and Liam Neeson all have houses in Millbrook, but they are among the newcomers. Residents, full time or part time, whose families have been in Millbrook for generations keep alive events like the Millbrook Hunt and the hunt ball. The Sandanona Hare Hounds is a group that sponsors weekly beagling: hounds chase the scent of cottontail rabbits and participants follow on foot. Every summer the Millbrook Horse Trials attract Olympics-caliber riders.
Other residents socialize in less tradition-bound ways.
Tim Smalls, an equity trader from Brooklyn Heights who bought a 19th-century colonial on 50 acres three years ago as his weekend home, takes his family to the July fishing derby at the town park and to free concerts at the town band shell. "It's great," he said. "The local plumber sits on a blanket drinking wine next to the C.E.O. of a Fortune 500 company."
Night life is minimal. "There's no big scene," said Mish Tworkowski, a jewelry designer from Manhattan. "That forces us to spend time at home with friends, which is a beautiful thing." Four years ago, he and his partner, Joseph Singer, an architect, bought a crumbling creamery and an adjacent barn, once part of a 19th-century estate, and combined them to create a 2,500-square-foot second home. "There were trees growing in the foundation walls," Mr. Singer said. Now their great room has 14-foot ceilings and original tongue-and-groove panels. 
An informal soccer league draws many foreign nationals living in town. "When the Argentinians play the Italians, it's fierce," said Elizabeth Baravalle, a store owner who moved to Millbrook from Milan in 1999.
Residents are proud of the recently renovated Millbrook Free Library; the capital campaign raised $2.5 million in eight months. In June, the Merritt Bookstore, which supports more than 10 book clubs and writing groups, will hold an overnight student read-athon to raise money for American Indian children nationwide.

Pros
Urbanites yearning for touches of small-town life can find them in Millbrook.
"We know everyone in every store by name," said Adelaide Camillo, an arts consultant for nonprofit groups and the wife of Mr. Gross, the corporate lawyer. The couple also catch up on local news during trips to the dump. "England has its pubs; we have the transfer station," Ms. Camillo said.
The village clerk, Karen McLaughlin, can send her sons, who are 10 and 13, out alone for ice cream without much worry. "Once the local police officer called to report my boys had crossed the road from between parked cars instead of at the corner," she said.
Gardeners abound. "Knock over a geranium pot, and you're one of Millbrook's most wanted," said Ms. Baravalle.
Many weekenders grow their own flowers and vegetables, and the Millbrook Farmers Market operates in the summer months. "I hit the ground on Friday afternoon with flats in my hand and that's it for the weekend," said Edith Kean, a landscape designer from Manhattan who has owned a second home in Millbrook for 16 years.
Many credit the Dutchess Land Conservancy with preserving open lands by acquiring conservation easements from local owners; under the easements, owners retain the land but relinquish development rights. The group has protected 4,600 acres in Washington this way, according to its executive director, Rebecca Thornton.
Children like the Trevor Zoo, which has more than 120 animals, including week-old red wolf pups. It is part of the Millbrook School, a private preparatory school where William F. Buckley Jr. was once a student. Wing's Castle, a private home with seven towers, a moat and mountain views built by Peter and Toni Wing using salvaged materials, is the site of an annual Shakespearean day camp for children. 
There are several popular restaurants in the village. Slammin' Salmon offers lunch daily, along with prepared food; the Cloud 9 Cafe serves breakfast and lunch; Cafe les Baux, a new French bistro, is open for lunch and dinner.

Cons
Millbrook may attract captains of industry, but Mother Nature still runs the ship. Spring is mud season. "We have wellies in the car and wellies by the front door," said Mr. Tworkowski. "If you live in your Manolo Blahniks, this is not the place for you."
Fresh produce is hard to find during winter, and long driveways that provide privacy can be difficult to clear after a large snowfall. During storms, power failures are frequent; many residents have their own generators.
While Lyme disease is prevalent in many parts of the Northeast, Dutchess County has one of the highest per capita rates in the country, and residents take every precaution during outdoor activities.
An air of exclusivity extends to a number of private shooting and sports clubs that have complex membership requirements and waiting lists of up to five years.

Real Estate Market
Housing prices rose nearly 23 percent from the end of 2000 until June 2002, when they leveled off, according to Peter Devers of George T. Whalen Real Estate (845-677-5076; www.gtwhalen.com). "The inventory in every price range is the lowest I've seen in my 22 years of selling real estate," Mr. Devers said. On the market in Millbrook and Washington this week were 39 single-family houses, five condominiums and town houses and seven parcels of undeveloped land.
This week, Whalen Real Estate offered a 1,500-square-foot two-bedroom cottage on 1.76 acres with a pond for $595,000 and a new four-bedroom, 3,162-square-foot house on 22 acres for $1.14 million. Diane Toland of H. W. Guernsey Realtors (845-677-5311, www.hwguernsey.com) predicted that the market would heat up along with the weather. Listings at Guernsey this week included a 2,125-square-foot split-level house with four bedrooms and views of the Catskills for $299,500 and a former home of Franklin D. Roosevelt Jr., a 6,571-square-foot shingled house with 10 bedrooms and a stable, pool and pond for $3.3 million.

LAY OF THE LAND
More Than Horses and Porsches

POPULATION: 4,742.
SIZE: 64 square miles.
NUMBER OF HOMES: 1,595.
MEDIAN HOUSE PRICE: $395,000.
RECENT SALES: Within the last four months, a three-bedroom, one-bathroom, 1,515-square-foot ranch on nearly an acre sold for $220,500 after 227 days on the market; a three-bedroom, two-bathroom, 3,150-square-foot hilltop house with a pond sold for $885,000 after 51 days; a four-bedroom, three-and-a-half-bathroom colonial-style farmhouse on nine and a half acres sold for $910,000 after 172 days; and a five-bedroom, five-bathroom, 4,400-square-foot colonial on 15 back-country acres sold for $1.3 million after being offered for 143 days.
DISTANCE FROM NEW YORK: 90 miles.
TRAVEL TIME: In light traffic, 90 minutes from New York. The Metro North express train from Grand Central Station takes 90 minutes to get to nearby Poughkeepsie.
GETTING THERE: Take the Taconic State Parkway north to the exit for Route 44, Millbrook, and follow Route 44 east into town.
WHILE YOU'RE LOOKING: A Cat in Your Lap (62 Old Route 82, 845-677-3051) is a comfortable bed-and-breakfast with two spacious rooms in a renovated 1840's barn and a small room off the main house, priced at $85 to $125 a night. The four rooms in the Millbrook Country House (506 Sharon Turnpike, Route 44A, 845-677-9570) are furnished with Italian antiques. Rates are $150 to $250 per night and include breakfast and afternoon tea.

HEADLINE: FILM REVIEW; 
Modern China's Clashing Values, Mirrored in a Prodigy's Career

BYLINE:  By STEPHEN HOLDEN 

 BODY:
Music, mostly of the Western classical variety and much of it floridly romantic, pours through Chen Kaige's film "Together" and helps infuse its story of a teenage violin prodigy and his devoted father with a feverish emotionalism. The newest movie by the Chinese filmmaker best known for "Farewell My Concubine" is not afraid to push buttons with its heady potpourri of Bruch, Richard Strauss, Liszt and Massenet, all pointing toward a climactic performance of the Tchaikovsky Violin Concerto in D.
 The grand musical swirl lends "Together" the feel of a historical epic, even though the movie is set almost entirely in contemporary Beijing and its screenplay barely alludes to the political and economic changes that have swept through China since the Cultural Revolution. The three major characters -- Xiaochun (Yun Tang); his father, Cheng Liu (Peiqi Liu ); and Lili (Hong Chen), a haughty, high-strung material girl who becomes both a fantasy figure and a surrogate mother to the boy -- are broadly drawn, almost symbolic figures in a movie that sheds more than one tear before it culminates in a weepy, inspirational cloudburst. 
 Characters who may at first seem selfish and hardhearted eventually reveal unexpected depths of generosity and nobility that, given what we know about them, requires some suspension of disbelief. This is a movie that insists people are good -- that when all is said and done they come through for one another when it really counts. The music insists upon it.
At the center of the story is Xiaochun, a shy 13-year-old violin prodigy who lives with his father in a provincial Chinese city. Hearing of a competition in Beijing that could be a stepping stone for his son, Liu, a short-order cook with few resources, takes the boy to the capital. The father worships his son's gift and eagerly abases himself in Xiaochun's behalf by badgering potential teachers to become his tutors.
But his persistence pays off. Xiaochun's first teacher, Professor Jiang (Zhiwen Wang), a grouchy eccentric who lives in squalor surrounded by his cats, treats his student as a housekeeper until the boy lashes back and expresses his disgust at Jiang's slovenly ways. Through his father's perseverance, Xiaochun eventually becomes one of two live-in students of Professor Yu (Chen Kaige), who is much more encouraging
The movie, which opens today in New York and Los Angeles, is nearly stolen by Hong Chen's hot-blooded portrait of Lili, an upstairs neighbor and would-be femme fatale who bewitches the adolescent musician. Beautiful, spirited and greedy, Lili is determined to marry money. But her potential husbands have other ideas, and her aggressive overtures are often cruelly rebuffed. Xiaochun earns tips from Lili by giving impromptu serenades as she prepares for her dates. In the story's turning point, he impulsively sells his violin to buy her a white fur-trimmed coat he observed her admiring in a dress shop.
Ms. Chen's Lili is a flaming creature who imperiously struts and sashays about and barks orders like a 1950's Hollywood floozy on the romantic warpath. The performance is so confident it almost reconciles the gaping contradictions of a character who seesaws between extremes of brassy self-promotion and tender solicitation.
Yun Tang's Xiaochun maintains an enigmatic reserve throughout the movie, which periodically drops clues as to what's seething under his placid, poker-faced facade. When Liu finds his son's violin case and sheet music plastered with pinups, it is suddenly clear that the boy's hormones are in full eruption. The selling of his violin is the culmination of Xiaochun's silent, deepening resentment toward a father whose obsequious groveling and devoted self-sacrifice have become a smothering embarrassment. But Xiaochun also has a solid gift and knows it, and as he moves through the film, Mr. Tang imbues him with an undercurrent of hauteur.
"Together" may not be overtly political, but its vision of contemporary Beijing, where brazen, fashion-crazed gold diggers like Lili bait their hooks to snare arrogant, slippery wheeler-dealers who end up playing her for a sucker, has bite. In the broad scheme of things, Liu represents the pure old ways, while Lili and her feckless boyfriends are the avid harbingers of a soulless but seductive consumer culture that is taking root. Xiaochun, torn between the two, is struggling like the rest of China to find a comfortable balance between aesthetic ideals and material temptations.
"Together" is rated PG (Parental guidance suggested). It has some strong language and adult themes.

TOGETHER
Directed by Chen Kaige; written (in Mandarin, with English subtitles) by Mr. Chen and Xiaolu Xue; director of photography, Hyungkoo Kim; edited by Ying Zhou; music by Lin Zhao, with violin solos by Chuanyun Li; production designers, Jiuping Cao and Luyi Liu; produced by Gang Ton and Hong Chen; released by United Artists. Running time: 117 minutes. This film is rated PG.

WITH: Peiqi Liu (Cheng Liu), Hong Chen (Lili), Zhiwen Wang (Professor Jiang), Chen Kaige (Professor Yu) and Yun Tang (Xiaochun).

HEADLINE: ON STAGE AND OFF 

BYLINE:  By Jesse McKinley

 BODY:
Small Show, Big Reaction
 Just when it seemed the season was casually meandering toward its inevitable conclusion, a tiny one-person show is causing an outsize ruckus on West 42nd Street. 
 That's the home of Playwrights Horizons -- at 416 West 42nd, to be exact -- where on Tuesday night the company opened a modest production of a new play by Doug Wright, "I Am My Own Wife," for a brief run right in the middle of Tony season. The show is simple: one actor, one set, one story, that of a German transvestite, Charlotte von Mahlsdorf, and life under the Nazi and East German regimes. 
The reaction to "Wife" has been anything but simple. Newspaper reviews on Wednesday ranged from good to excellent, with particular praise for the show's little-known star, Jefferson Mays, who plays dozens of characters in von Mahlsdorf's life. Writing in The New York Times, Bruce Weber said Mr. Mays delivered "an extraordinary performance," adding that audiences were "riveted by his commitment to the character's self-knowledge and proud eccentricity."
Those notices have attracted attention both inside Playwrights and beyond. The show, which was scheduled to run only two weeks, has been extended through July 20, and commercial producers are circling for the rights to take it elsewhere. The production is selling "the best of any show in years," said Tim Sanford, the company's artistic director.
Nothing is set, but Mr. Sanford seemed confident that "Wife" would have a life. "There were producers interested before it opened, and that interest is still there," Mr. Sanford said, adding that both Broadway and Off Broadway had been bandied about as possible future sites. The producers have also asked that Mr. Mays, who has worked extensively in regional theater and with downtown directors like Anne Bogart, keep his schedule open for a while. One of Mr. Mays's first big roles was in another Doug Wright play, "Quills," in 1995.
The show, directed by Moises Kaufman ("Gross Indecency: The Three Trials of Oscar Wilde"), seems particularly popular among theater professionals, and many were at the opening. One early fan was Gerald Schoenfeld, chairman of the Shubert Organization, who advised his troops last week to check out the production. Other producers said to be interested include the San Francisco-based heiress Carole Shorenstein Hays, ("Take Me Out") and Susan Gallin, now represented by "Man of La Mancha."
One obvious appeal for a commercial producer -- besides the art, it's always about the art -- is that it would be very affordable (no pyrotechnics, a really small cast).
The timing is also in the company's favor. The show is playing in a new 200-seat theater that has nothing else booked until fall, so Playwrights can take a nice long time to decide and maybe have another extension. "It's not a pressing urgency," Mr. Sanford said, "which is nice, because summer can be a very slow time in the theater."

A Record, and Not
A little bookkeeping and record-breaking: The League of American Theaters and Producers said yesterday that it had tallied the year's sales and that it was estimating that the Broadway gross would be more than $720.9 million for the 2002-3 season, which ends Sunday. (The season runs for 52 weeks from the last Tony Awards; last year's awards were June 2.)
That's an all-time high, said the league's president, Jed Bernstein, and a 12 percent increase over last season, which was rough because of the slow economy and Sept. 11. But while the gross is a record, actual attendance was not, indicating that Broadway ticket prices continue to rise. Estimated attendance will be 11.4 million, just shy of the 11.9 million record in the 2000-1 season.

The Next Season
Broadway, land of the musical, confirmed another new straight play for the fall, joining the previously announced Richard Greenberg's "Violet Hour," expected at the Biltmore, and "Six Dance Lessons in Six Weeks,"due at a theater to named.
It is "Retreat From Moscow," a family drama by William Nicholson that played at the Chichester Festival in England in 2000 and was briefly rumored in May 2001 to be moving to Broadway with Mike Nichols as its director and producer and a cast including Ralph Fiennes, Sir Anthony Hopkins and Sinead Cusack. Those plans never developed, however, and by the end of that year another producer, Susan Gallin, was in the mix.
Now, Ms. Gallin and Stuart Thompson plan to open the play at the Booth Theater on Oct. 23 under the direction of Daniel Sullivan, who has directed "Proof" and "A Moon for the Misbegotten" in recent years. He is close to announcing his cast, including John Lithgow (who won the Tony for best actor in a musical last year in "Sweet Smell of Success"), the fine Eileen Atkins and Ben Chaplin, the 32-year-old British actor.
Mr. Chaplin, who has performed on the West End and in film ("Murder by Numbers," "The Truth About Cats and Dogs"), will play the son of the Lithgow and Atkins characters, a couple whose 35-year marriage is deteriorating.

Along the Way
* Gale Gates et al., an always provocative, often oblique experimental company that was a trailblazer in Brooklyn, is calling it quits. The eight-year-old company produced and presented nearly 60 multimedia events (naked people, awesome lights, strange prose), many conceived by its artistic director, Michael Counts, and all staged in the company's 40,000-square-foot space. Michelle Stern, the company's producing director, said the troupe was folding after an extended search for an executive director proved fruitless. The company will celebrate its demise with a bash-performance event on July 12.
* The Aquila Theater Company, a classically trained, modernly hip troupe that did a well-received version of "Comedy of Errors" last summer, returns this year with a spiced up 1970's version of Oscar Wilde's "Importance of Being Earnest." Performances begin June 21 at the Baruch Performing Arts Center, 55 Lexington Avenue, at East 25th Street, Manhattan.

HEADLINE: DRIVING; 
Where Adults Go to Play In the Mud

BYLINE:  By DANNY HAKIM 

DATELINE: SOUTH BEND, Ind. 

 BODY:
LIKE most of the students at Hummer camp, Maria del Carmen Grimmelmann tends to gush. "It moves me," Mrs. Grimmelmann, 51, said of the supercharged Hummer H2 she and her husband, Frank, bought earlier this year.
 "You know when you go shopping and nothing moves you?" she asked. "Then there's the time you see something, and right away know it's perfect. It's like falling in love. When I'm driving it, I feel empowered. It's the car that opens the sea for me. Now I know how Moses felt." 
 Every few years a vehicle comes along that draws a crowd, but few have been loved or hated with such vigor as the Hummer. To environmentalists, it is a gas-gulping, globe-warming menace. To consumer safety advocates, a highway battering ram. But to many of its customers, the Hummer is the first American car to stir emotion in decades. 
And for the truly smitten, there is Hummer camp, a sort of 21st-century dude ranch of mud-besotted excess more formally known as the Hummer Driving Academy. For three nights and two days last week, half a dozen students convened for a camp session, rambling over obstacle courses and roaring through 300 acres of muddy terrain thickly wooded with beech, white oak, cherry and shag-bark hickory trees in South Bend. The fee for this privilege is $3,575, plus $875 to bring along a spouse. 
The campers were from far-flung locales and backgrounds. Among them, for example, were Red Barley, 64, a retired Defense Department engineer from Severna Park, Md.; Major Chance, 58, a San Diego entrepreneur in the retirement housing business; Gary Hobbs, a 55-year-old Ferrari dealer from Oakton, Va.; and Mark Mills, 52, an unemployed former Verizon worker from Ohio.
Wait. How can Mr. Mills afford a $48,000 Hummer H2?
He won an $11 million Ohio lottery jackpot in 2001. And as the basketball star Lebron James demonstrated, buying a Hummer is de rigueur for mega-nouveau riche Ohioans.
But for Hummer skeptics, two questions linger: Who buys this thing? And why?
We need answers. To Hummer camp!
Once a private hunting ground for South Bend's last auto dynasty, the Studebakers, the woodsy campgrounds are now run by AM General, which manufactures Hummers and the military transports they are patterned after, Humvees. (General Motors bought the rights to market Hummers in 1999 and designed the H2.) On the edge of the woods, a modest, warehouse-style classroom complex is surrounded by an obstacle course that contains pits filled with water, boulders or mud.
Hundreds of students pass through the camp each year. Some are members of the Army's Special Forces units, learning to drive Humvees. Others are Hummer owners; this year classes have been added for the new H2. 
Even the Hummer owners drive the camp's H2's rather than their own. That gives them license to run risks they might never take with their own trucks. (Hummer drivers don't typically drive off-road -- that's what Hummer camp is for.)
"This is too nice a truck to do this to," said Mr. Mills, the jowly, mustachioed lottery winner, appraising a camp loaner that was covered with dirt and scratches after becoming so bogged down in mud that it took a winch to pull it free. "They said they had their own trucks. I was like, 'I'll come.' "
What's the appeal of Hummer camp? Uncomplicated. Take the proverbial boy in the sandbox. Add a few decades, a few pounds, some gray hair, a global positioning device and plenty of disposable income. Throw in oil, water and 6,400 pounds of steel, rubber and leather. And time to bond with like-minded souls (some of whom may even turn out to be women).
Scenes from Hummer camp:

WEDNESDAY, AFTER LUNCH -- Mark Mills and David Paschen, a 50-year-old recreational vehicle dealer from Chesterton, Ind., acquaintances for all of one day, are barreling up and down mud hills following a caravan of Hummers. Mr. Paschen is playing befuddled straight man while Mr. Mills doles out zingers.
Why did you buy a Hummer, Dave?
"I turned 50, and I decided if I was ever going to do it, this was the time," Mr. Paschen replied.
Mr. Mills interjects, "His wife said he could."
Mark, why did you buy a Hummer?
Well, he had a BMW sports car and had considered an H1, but there were complications.
"I don't want to say I'm a fat guy," he explains. "But I'm a fat guy. The seats in that H1 aren't very big." Besides, the H1 was twice the H2's price.
"I only have so much money," Mr. Mills said. Kidding!

3:20 P.M. -- Their Hummer is inching along a narrow downhill trail strewn with what seem like ski-slope moguls.
Mr. Mills: "Dave likes the brake. Dave won't take a foot off the brake. Oh man, Dave, you're never going to make it through this."
Mr. Paschen: "We'll make it." He does not sound convinced, but they clear the hill after a jarring ride.

4:20 P.M. -- Classroom time. The Chances (Major and Judy) of San Diego are sitting in front of the Grimmelmanns (Frank and Maria) of Anthem, Ariz., and all are trying to find local landmarks on nifty global positioning devices that have been handed to them.
"No wonder this course costs so much," says Mr. Mills, from the back.
Mr. Paschen, next to him: "What's Cleveland doing here? I think we're in trouble." 
Mr. Chance notes that he has the coordinates of a bar.
"I already found it," Mr. Mills says.

5:12 P.M. -- Seven students are outside trying to acquire satellite signals with their G.P.S. units, then amble in zigzag patterns toward a Dumpster. It's a test. They'll need this skill later for the Hummer Olympics. (That's another story, but suffice it to say Mr. Paschen and Mr. Mills finished last and had particular trouble with the blind Hummer driving test and balancing their H2 on a giant seesaw.)

THE NEXT MORNING -- Judy Chance and Maria Grimmelmann are on the practice course. Before setting out, Mrs. Chance, the driver, momentarily rests her blond head on the steering wheel, sunglasses in place, as Mrs. Grimmelmann, in the back seat, makes the sign of the cross. Ahead, a concrete barrier looms, followed by a short hill sloped at a 31-degree angle, in the manner of the highway turnoffs sometimes cut into mountainsides to corral runaway trucks.
"Very gently, I'd like you to touch the wall," Timothy Bonadies, the 46-year-old course designer and manager of the camp, says from the front passenger seat. He has learned to coax leaps of faith gently from the gravity-bound.
Slowly, they nudge over the 16-inch concrete barrier, tire by tire, and approach the slope.
"Roll forward," says Mr. Bonadies, whoserves as a sort of Zen master. "Approach it like your driveway at home."
"Oh my God," says Mrs. Chance, as the behemoth eases up the slope and points toward the blue sky. "I can't see anything."
"Roll up and over," Mr. Bonadies says. "Straight ahead."
The truck clears the slope, only to find another, angled steeply sideways, which causes the right side to soar while the left dips to a mere arm's length from the ground. While Mrs. Chance leans over the gear shift to fight the sensation that she is about to tumble out her window, Mr. Bonadies is perched high above her.
His voice remains serene. 
It won't help you to lean this way," he says.
Later, Mrs. Grimmelmann observed: "You can't be afraid. If you let fear take over, that's where you fall into mishaps. This vehicle really brings out that inner strength you didn't know you had."
Truck drivers used to intimidate her to the point that she would pull off the road to let them pass.
"But when I'm in the Hummer, they leave me alone," she said. "They just go away. And I like that."
Mr. Chance, a man with the erect bearing and basso voice of a somewhat younger Charlton Heston, explained the Hummer appeal over dinner. 
"The last G.M. car I liked was a '57 Chevy," he said. "I think it's so important that car designers create passion. Europe does it -- BMW, Mercedes, Ferrari. They create passion and make you willing to write a check. There's no logic. If I had sat down and done a cost-benefit analysis, I would have been, 'No-o-o.' Of course, I took delivery in California, and gas went to $2.50. So that's a statement of passion or stupidity, I don't know which."
This is why G.M. executives, beleaguered by foreign competition, love the Hummer. To them, it is a premium-price miracle that sells without the huge incentives needed to move Pontiacs, Oldsmobiles, Buicks and even Cadillacs.
"Having grown up in G.M., it's kind of like getting a winning lottery ticket," Marc Hernandez, Hummer's marketing director, said of his current job. He can remember working at G.M. divisions that aroused anything but passion. 
"I know what it's like to hear the crickets," he said, "and watch the tumbleweed roll across my desk."

THE DETAILS
If Hummer Camp Beckons (or if It Doesn't)
THE Hummer Driving Academy, open only to owners of Hummers, is in South Bend, Ind., the home of Notre Dame and of AM General, the company that builds Humvees for the military and Hummers for General Motors customers. Here is the vital information for owners who want to go.

TO REGISTER -- Call (866) 831-9547 for the H2 camp and 574-258-6622 for the H1 camp, or go to www.hummer.com. Schedules can be found at the Calendar tab in the Hummer World section.

WHAT IT COSTS -- For H1 camp, which lasts four days and five nights, $5,250 per student and $1,500 to bring along a friend or spouse. You get a $350 discount if you share a room. For H2 camp, which lasts two days and three nights, $3,575 per student, $875 for a friend or spouse, with a $150 discount for room-sharing. Meals, lodging and Hummers are included.

WHAT TO BRING -- Hiking boots and clothes that are mud-friendly. Casual dress for dinners.

IF THE VERY IDEA ALARMS YOU -- If the thought of going to Hummer camp turns you a darker shade of green, membership information for the Sierra Club -- which is planning a campaign against Hummers -- is available at www.sierraclub.org, or by calling (415) 977-5653.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS; 
Standard & Poor's Index Falls, Ending a Five-Day Rally

BYLINE:  By Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell for the first trading day in six yesterday as some investors said its rally to a nine-month high already reflected expected gains in earnings.
 "We've probably seen the strongest part of the advance," said Thomas Madden, who oversees $185 billion in investments as market strategist at Federated Investors in Pittsburgh. "Investors will justifiably want to wait to see if there is good news on the economy and corporate profits grow." 
 Eastman Kodak was a drag on the Dow Jones industrial average after an analyst said declining photo-processing sales at a Kodak customer, Costco Wholesale, suggested slowing revenue for the company. 
The S.& P. 500 dropped 3.58 points, or 0.4 percent, to 949.64, while the Dow average lost 81.94 points, or 0.9 percent, to 8,711.18. Both snapped a five-day winning streak, their longest rally in more than two months.
The Nasdaq composite index advanced 11.71 points, or 0.8 percent, to 1,574.95 as computer-related shares climbed.
On Wednesday, the S.& P. 500 closed at its highest since Aug. 22 after reports showed gains in new-home sales and consumer confidence. A government report yesterday showed jobless claims stayed above 400,000 for a 15th week, a sign the economy is too weak to increase employment growth.
"The market has had a big move and the jobless numbers weren't good," said Jay Suskind, head of institutional equity trading at Ryan Beck & Company in New Jersey. "Maybe you want to cut back on your gainers."
Kodak dropped 82 cents, to $30.01. Photo-processing results at Costco, which handles as much as 15 percent of film processing in the United States under an exclusive contract with Kodak, fell in the third quarter, suggesting a sales decline for Kodak, Craig Ellis, a Smith Barney analyst, said in a note to clients.
Costco fell 72 cents, to $36.63. A Morgan Stanley analyst, Bruce Missett, downgraded the company, the large warehouse-club store chain, to equal-weight from overweight. He said the shares, which are up 31 percent since March 11, were fairly priced relative to earnings.
J. C. Penney dropped 59 cents, to $16.61. Standard & Poor's cut the company's debt to a junk rating.
Technology shares gained, lifting the Nasdaq for a fifth day, on expectations that companies that make computers, software and related equipment would be among the first beneficiaries of growth in the economy.
Companies in the S.& P. 500 Information Technology Index are expected to report a 30 percent earnings increase this year, according to a Thomson First Call survey of analysts. That is more than twice as fast the growth rate for all S.& P. 500 members.
Intel, the world's largest semiconductor maker, rose $1.09, to $20.83. Cisco Systems, the biggest maker of equipment to direct Internet traffic, added 34 cents, to $16.62.
Treasury bond prices rose, after a government report showed jobless claims stayed above 400,000 for a 15th week, a sign the economy is too weak to increase employment growth.
Demand for government debt rose as some investors speculated Federal Reserve policy makers might cut their target interest rate of 1.25 percent. 
A new two-year note at auction was priced to yield 1.305 percent. In when-issued trading by late in the day the yield was 1.303 percent.
The benchmark 10-year note rose 23/32, to a price of 102 13/32. The note's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, dropped to 3.34 percent from 3.43 percent.
And the 30-year bond climbed 1 6/32, to 116 19/32. Its yield fell to 4.34 percent from 4.41 percent.

HEADLINE: Residential Real Estate; 
Co-ops Are Less Likely to Reject Buyers, Study Says

BYLINE:  By DENNIS HEVESI 

 BODY:
Co-op boards are less likely to become entangled in lawsuits, less likely to reject prospective apartment buyers and more likely to trust building managers than they were in the mid-90's, when the co-op and condominium conversion movement was just passing its peak, according to a citywide sampling of the views of 200 board presidents.
 "What we're seeing is a maturation process; the boards have learned a great deal about running buildings," said Richard Siegler, a partner in the law firm of Stroock & Stroock & Lavan. Mr. Siegler along with Eva Talel, his colleague in the law firm's co-op and condo division, conducted the 48-question survey -- a follow-up to a similar study in 1996. 
 At least one prominent industry observer wondered whether the sampling was big enough and said he felt there might actually have been an increase in lawsuits. 
The 2003 study found, for example, that 71 percent of the buildings that responded had filed no lawsuits against apartment owners in the previous two years. Almost the opposite was true in 1996, when 67 percent of the boards reported filing suit against at least one apartment owner during the previous two years.
"That's a wow!" Ms. Talel said. "And it speaks to the maturation process, where boards are smarter, have experienced the costs and disruptions of litigation and are engaging in alternative dispute resolution -- good old-fashioned trying to work it out."
The same might be said of apartment owners. The current survey found that 83 percent of the boards had not been sued by owners in the last two years, while 54 percent of the boards in 1996 had not faced legal action during the previous two years.
The current study also found that 96 percent of the board presidents say that the companies that manage their buildings deal honestly with the buildings. "This increased from 88 percent in 1996," Mr. Siegler said, "suggesting limited fallout from the 1994 investigation of corruption in residential housing." The investigation, by the Manhattan district attorney, Robert M. Morgenthau, "was Morgenthau's attempt to crack down on bribery between building managers and vendors," Mr. Siegler said, with buildings paying inflated contract costs to cover the bribes.
Ms. Talel said the 2003 study -- in which about half of the 200 respondents were in Manhattan -- went on to indicate that co-op boards were not as arbitrary as commonly perceived when it came to rejecting buyers. "We found in 2003 that for 66 percent of the buildings, fewer than 1 percent of the sales were turned down," she said. "And in '96, it was 56 percent. So you're seeing a decline in sales being disapproved." 
The survey delved into several financial issues. It found that 51 percent of the buildings had seen insurance costs jump by at least half since 2000, with 11 percent more than doubling. In 1996, 45 percent of the buildings required owners to post a security deposit if they were undertaking apartment alterations; by last year, that had increased to 70 percent.
In 1996, 13 percent of the buildings had more than 10 percent of their apartment owners in arrears on their monthly payments; by 2003, only 1 percent of the buildings had more than 10 percent of their apartments in arrears.
Noting that it was "difficult to extrapolate" results from 200 buildings for the 6,000 co-ops and condos in the city, Stuart M. Saft, chairman of the Council of New York Cooperatives and Condominiums, said he thought there were now more lawsuits.
But the survey does provide "valuable additional information that confirms, to some degree, things we have seen," Mr. Saft added. "For instance, we believe that approximately 50 percent of cooperatives now have flip taxes, and the report indicates that 62 percent have them, confirming the numbers we have been using." Flip taxes -- fees paid by either the buyer or seller to the co-op when an apartment is sold -- have proliferated in a time of increased maintenance charges caused by higher property taxes, insurance and labor costs.
Mr. Saft noted that in the last few months, as co-op and condo boards held their annual elections: "We have seen more proxy fights for board control than at any time over the last 10 years. Many of these buildings are seemingly well run, so I think it's just the reaction of residents to those increased operating costs and the difficulty in the economy."

HEADLINE: City Unemployment Rate Drops; Statewide, Job Numbers Edge Up 

BYLINE:  By JANNY SCOTT 

 BODY:
The unemployment rate in New York City dropped in April for the second time in two straight months, this time to 8.3 percent from 8.5 percent, according to monthly data released yesterday by the State Department of Labor. The number of private-sector jobs grew more than it had in many months.
 The city comptroller, William C. Thompson Jr., said private-sector jobs in the city had increased by 10,700, when seasonally adjusted. He said the increase amounted to the largest single-month gain since September 2000. 
 Job gains were reported in construction, financial activities, education and health services, with losses in leisure and hospitality and in manufacturing.
Statewide, the number of private-sector jobs grew by 9,400 after seasonal adjustment, according to the Labor Department.
Several economists said the new numbers were somewhat encouraging. Some said they showed that the state economy was slowly recovering from the national recession, the troubled state of Wall Street and the Sept. 11, 2001, attacks. In New York City, they said, the numbers suggest a leveling off after many months of decline.
"When things are bad, a little bit of good news is encouraging," said Jason Bram, an economist with the Federal Reserve Bank of New York. "The March numbers were looking bad. Now it looks a lot better. Not a boom, but a lot better. For now."
Stephen Kagann, the chief economist for Gov. George E. Pataki, said in a statement: "The data show the New York State economy is slowly improving. Most regions are gaining jobs or are stronger than the nation, and the regions in decline are declining at a diminishing rate."
Employment growth in the New York City suburbs and upstate has outpaced that of the nation as a whole over the past year, Mr. Kagann said. Job losses have been concentrated in New York City, Rochester and Binghamton, but the rate of decline has slowed, he added.
Jim Brown, a labor market analyst for the state, said the new numbers showed "above-average strength" in construction and employment services. Those services, which include temporary agencies, are considered a barometer of business conditions.
Mr. Brown said the decline in New York City's April unemployment rate could be traced to actual employment gains rather than to a drop in the number of people seeking jobs, as had been the case in March.
The state's unemployment rate rose to 6.1 percent from 6.0 percent.

HEADLINE: 1st-Quarter Growth Is Revised To a 1.9%Pace, Still Sluggish 

BYLINE:  AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 29 

 BODY:
The economy grew a bit faster in the first three months of 2003 than initially thought, the Commerce Department reported today, but the advance was still considered mediocre.
 A revision in the gross domestic product showed that economic growth increased at an annual rate of 1.9 percent in the quarter, modestly stronger than the 1.6 percent growth rate estimated a month ago. 
 Economists said a growth rate around 3 percent or higher was needed to return to a more normal pattern and persuade companies to start hiring. "Until that happens, it won't feel much like an economic recovery to the average person," said Carl Tannenbaum, chief economist at LaSalle Bank.
The Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, testified last week that growth this quarter "is going to be quite soft."
Many private economists do not expect the economy in this period to be much better than in January through March. Forecasts for second-quarter growth range from a 1 percent pace to more than 2 percent.
Economists are optimistic that activity will pick up in the second half of the year, with some predicting annual growth around 4 percent. "We think the recent rise in stock prices is already foreshadowing this revival," said Maury Harris of UBS Warburg.
A big reason for the higher first-quarter G.D.P. reading was that consumers spent a little more than previously thought.
Separately, new claims for unemployment benefits dropped last week by a seasonally adjusted 9,000, to 424,000, the Labor Department said. Even with the decline, claims were above the 400,000 level, which is associated with a weak job market. The four-week moving average fell to 427,000 from 434,250.

HEADLINE: THREATS AND RESPONSES: LAW ENFORCEMENT; 
U.S. Cautiously Begins to Seize Millions in Foreign Banks

BYLINE:  By ERIC LICHTBLAU 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 29 

 BODY:
The Justice Department has begun using its expanded counterterrorism powers to seize millions of dollars from foreign banks that do business in the United States, creating tensions with the State Department and some allies.
 Law enforcement officials say the tool has proved invaluable in seizing ill-gotten money that criminals hide overseas and that was once out of the government's reach. Under the counterterrorism measures approved by Congress after the Sept. 11 attacks, prosecutors are not even required to trace the money back to the target of an investigation. 
 Officials at the State Department, however, have raised concerns over the practice, in part because most of the seizures have involved fraud and money-laundering investigations that are unrelated to terrorism.
State Department officials worry "that this might be seen by other countries as arbitrary or trying to extra-territorially impose our laws" under the guise of fighting terrorism, said a Bush administration official who demanded anonymity. Diplomats from several nations, including Switzerland, have voiced private objections, officials said. 
The Justice Department has seized at least 15 foreign-based bank accounts in the United States in recent months, confiscating what prosecutors say they believe to be tainted money belonging to overseas banks in Israel, Oman, Taiwan, India, Belize and elsewhere, said law enforcement officials who spoke on condition of anonymity. Other seizures are also being considered, officials said. 
Justice Department officials acknowledged the diplomatic problems that can be created by seizing money from foreign banks, and they said they have sought to use the new power sparingly after considering all other legal options. 
The authority to seize ill-gotten foreign funds "is a very powerful tool and one that can affect our international relationships," said Bryan Sierra, a spokesman for the Justice Department. 
Mr. Sierra said that to protect the law enforcement relationships the United States has developed with governments around the world, the Justice Department "scrutinizes prosecutors' use of this provision before it is exercised."
The Justice Department has sent out guidance to prosecutors around the country about how to use the new power and has reached an informal understanding with the State Department to consult more extensively with officials there before seizing foreign money. State Department officials had complained that the Justice Department had kept them out of the loop in some cases.
"This is a process of last resort," said a senior law enforcement official. American diplomats "are worried about it, and we understand that, which is why we want to use this judiciously," said the official.
The State Department declined to comment on the issue. But a department official who spoke on condition of anonymity acknowledged that there was still the potential for abuse in foreign seizures, despite the Justice Department's pledges to consider diplomatic issues in its decisions.
"From a diplomatic point of view, we've got concerns," the official said. "We're the ones who have to ensure diplomatic relations, and this complicates that."
Traditionally in money laundering and terror financing cases, federal authorities have worked through international law enforcement treaties in requesting that a country that is home to a foreign bank move to freeze "dirty money" and turn it over to the United States.
In countries with no treaty with the United States, American authorities say, their efforts often ran into dead ends. 
But a little-noticed provision in the sweeping antiterrorism legislation passed in October 2001, gave federal authorities in such cases the power to seize money that passes through banks in the United States without notifying the foreign government. Most overseas banks maintain what are called "correspondent accounts" in American banks, allowing them to exchange American currency and handle other financial transactions in this country. Section 319 of the Patriot Act, as the legislation that grew out of the Sept. 11 attacks is known, allows federal authorities to seize money from the foreign bank's correspondent account if they can convince a judge that the money deposited overseas at the bank was obtained illicitly.
Information about the seizures has been tightly guarded, and federal judges have sealed the records on most of them. The Justice Department acknowledged two cases in which the authorities have seized a total of more than $2 million from foreign banks but declined to give the total number of seizures. 
Law enforcement officials said the Justice Department had employed the new tool in about a half-dozen investigations, seizing money from at least 15 bank accounts. Most of those came in recent months and involved fraud and laundering cases, officials said. Law enforcement officials said some of the seizures have involved money that they suspect was helping to finance terrorism, but they declined to discuss details. Officials also see the measure as a potentially powerful tool in seizing money from drug traffickers.
In a case that resulted in a guilty plea in federal court this week, federal authorities seized $310,000 from four banks in the New York City area that held correspondent accounts with foreign banks in Oman, India, and Taiwan.
In seizing the money, the authorities charged that a Sudanese citizen in Brooklyn, Ahmed Abdu, had illegally wired more than $5 million to bank accounts around the world. It was the first time the seizure tool had been used by federal prosecutors in New York's Southern District. 
Mr. Abdu pleaded guilty on Wednesday to a charge of conspiring to run an unlicensed money transmitting business and faces up to five years in prison. The authorities seized the $310,000 from four banks in the New York City area that held correspondent accounts on behalf of overseas banks: Citibank, Standard Chartered Bank, Deutsche Bank, and HSBC Bank USA.
"It was a strange thing for the banks," said a financial investigator with the Bureau of Immigration and Customs Enforcement in New York who spoke on condition of anonymity. "There was resistance initially, because they weren't exactly sure what to do. They'd never seen something like this before."
The seizure power has opened up doors once closed in financial crime investigations, the investigator said. "Now we can get ahold of money where we couldn't before," he said. "But we proceed very cautiously. It's such a powerful tool, you could knock the economy of a country on its head if it were a big enough case."
A seizure in Belize, centering on a lawyer and financial manager who was accused of embezzling money from his clients, has become a model for how the law can be applied, officials said. 
The lawyer, James Gibson, was accused of bilking clients out of millions of dollars and then fleeing to Central America. Prosecutors said that Mr. Gibson, indicted by a federal grand jury in 2001, deposited some of the money in banks in Belize.
Although the government of Belize initially agreed to freeze the money, a court there blocked the move, and prosecutors said they believed that Mr. Gibson and his wife were looting the accounts to buy yachts and other luxury items. 
But after the passage of the Patriot Act, the Justice Department moved within weeks in late 2001 to seize the money from the Belizean banks' correspondent accounts in the United States, according to a report prosecutors gave Congress last week on the new law enforcement powers.
Officials said that the money seized from Belize totaled about $1.7 million, and that it would be used to compensate Mr. Gibson's victims.
Charles A. Intriago, a specialist in money laundering law in Miami who publishes Money Laundering Alert, said he considered the Justice Department's new power to seize foreign bank accounts "maybe the most startling provision of the Patriot Act." He said: "It's an awesome power, and it may even go too far because of the diplomatic problems it could cause."
But Mr. Intriago added that he did not fault the Justice Department for considering the new tool in a range of fraud, laundering and drug trafficking cases that are unrelated to their counterterrorism mandate.
"If Congress had wanted this tool to be limited to terrorism, it would have done so," he said. "It didn't."

HEADLINE: Technology Briefing Software: J. D. Edwards Posts 2nd-Quarter Loss 

BYLINE:  Reuters 

 BODY:
 The business software maker J. D. Edwards & Company posted a loss for the second quarter yesterday versus a profit a year earlier, as revenues dropped in the weak economy. The company said that it recorded growth in the number of customers, but that its transactions were smaller. 
For the quarter ended April 30, it posted a net loss of $393,000, compared with a profit of $3.5 million, or 3 cents a share, in the year-ago quarter. The Denver-based company said that on a pro forma basis, excluding unusual costs, it earned a profit of $1.1 million, or 1 cent a share, compared with a profit of $7.6 million, or 6 cents a share, in the year-ago quarter. Total revenue declined to $203.5 million from $223.6 million the year before, the company said. License revenues fell to $43.6 million from $54.1 million a year earlier, while service revenues declined to $160 million from $169.5 million.
